FUJIAN PROVINCIAL HIGHWAY PROJECT
LoAN     PROJECT SummRY
Borrower:          People's Republic of China
Beefciary:         Fujian Province
Amount:            $140 million equivalent
Terms:             20 years, including S years of grace, at the Bank's standard variable
interest rate
Project Objectives: The project has been designed to (a) support the continued
development of road infrastructure in Fujian Province to facilitate
mobility and increase the efficiency of ro4d transport in the interest
of stimulating economic activity; (b) develop an action plan for the
strengthening of the provincial road construction industry; (c) assist
in the development of institutional capabilities for transport planning
and investment prioritization; and (d) ficilitate the transfer of
highway construction and maintenance technology through staff
training and the provision of equipment.
Projet Dsripton: The project comprises:  (a) the construction of the Xiamen-
Quanzhou four-lane divided, access-controlled highway (81.4 kn);
(b) a Road Network Improvement Ptogram (RN1P), including the
upgraang (58.3 EIn) and paving (202.7 kn) of seven rural arterial
roads; (c) construction supervision of the Xiamen-Quanzhou
Highway and the civil works included in the RNIP; (d) purchase of
equipment for highway operation and maintenance, road
laboratories, a road data bank and a pavement management system,
(e) a study of the provincial road construcdon industry; and (f) staff
taning.
Project Beneits:    The main quantifiable benefits associated with development of the
highway and the seven rural arterial roads include savings in vehicle
operating costs, travel time, and road maintenance costs, and
savings through reduction of taffic accidents. Benefits from the
projects would accrue to road users, including the operators of
freight and passenger transport services, enterprises and farmers, in
the form of reduced vehicle operating costs. The Province would
This document has a rstricted disttibution and may be used by tecipients only in the performance
Iof their ofciaW duties. !ts contents may not otherwise be disclose without World Bank authorization.



-ii
benefit from reduced cost of road maintenance, and a broad
segment of the community would benefit from the reduction in
traffic accidents. Benefits, particularly from the RNIP, would
accrue from improved access to remote areas through fostering
accelerated economic growth and greater mobility for social
activities. Institutional benefits accrue from the involvement of
local personnel in project preparation, requiring partcularly
rigorous treatment of economic and environmental assessments,
engineering design, technical specifications and construction
practices. Such exposure will provide benefits far beyond the
implementation peiod of the proposed project through the
expence gained by the persomnnel involved. The specific attention
given to the road construction industry in Fujian Province to
encourage its transition to market-orientated operations is also an
initiative with good potential for long-term benefits.
Pisks:            Based on the experience with ongoing highway projects, the main
risk i implementation delay, paricularly for the highway
component. Because of the difficult subsoil conditions along some
of the highway alignment, the relatively high number of tunnels and
bridges to be constructed and the extensive rock excavation
involved, there are major risks of delay in the timely completion of
the highway. Since the Fujian Province is a first-time borrower for
a major Bank-financed highway project, the lack of experience in
executing major Bank-financed highway projects adds an element
of risk to smooth project implementation. However, project
preparation has benefitted significantly from the Bank's previous
expeience in similar highway projects in China, the participation
of foreign experts in the design of the highway and the preparation
of the bidding documents and technical specifications, the stringent
prequalificaton of contractors, and the careful fonnulation of
supervision to ensure regular monitoring and effective quality
control.

1.               SPORT SECTR
A. IRODUCfON
1.1        The transport system in China has grown considerably over the last four
decades. Between 1952 and 1992, the lengti of the highway system increased by a factor
of nine and the lngt of the railway network more than doubled.  Despite this
development, the transport network is one of the sparsest in the world compared with the
area and populton it serves (Table 1.1). The main reason for this is China's low level
of tasport investment, which averaged only 1.3 percnt of GNP during 19892 (ables
1.2 and 1.3), as compared with 2-3 percent for other countries such as India, the Republic
of Korea and Brazil.
1.2        The transport system is also chracterized by a high intensity of freight
traffic. To produce one dollar of economic output, China handles 50 prcent more ton-
klometers (ton-km) of taffic than India and Brzi (Table 1.1). The major reasns behind
China's high freight traffic intensity are a rlatively large heavy-industry sector, reliance
on coal as the major source of energy and a large volume of other bulk products. These
fators have resuted in a gap between transport supply and demand, which is manifested
by the eitence of tranwport botleneclk, the adminive rationing of limited tansport
capacity, the poor quality of sevices and losses to the economy.
1.3        The economic system in China is undergoing a major transition tward a
market orientation and this transition will involve many reforms in the tnsport sctor,
designed to increase efficiency and improve its response to market forces. These reforms,
which std  in 1979, are now acceleating due to government policies announced in 1992.
D. TRAlC
1.4        Since the adoption of an ope-door policy in 1979, China has exerienced
rapd growth of tfic, consistent ith fast expansion of the economy, which averaged
about 9.5 prcent a year during 1980-90. This growth trend tpered off in recent years
as the supply constraints became more conspicuous and the growth of the economy slowed
down in 1989/90. However, resumption of high economic growth since 1991 has brought
about   rponding surge in tansport demand, which has staied the capity of the
transpot system.
1.5        Traf   Growth. Despite capaty constints and related bottlenecks,
during 1982-92 total freight traffic grew at 8.1 percent per annum altd total intercity
pasenger traffic at 9.7 percent per annum. Highway freight taffic grew at the  gest
annual rate, 14.7 percent, compared with 6.6 percent fo railway taffic and 9.5 pcnt



- 2 -
for coastal and inland waterway taffic C(able 1.4). Despite its rapid growth, intercity
passenger travel per capita in China remains very low, at about two thirds of the level for
India. This is due primarily to severe capacity constraints. Civil aviation traffic, which
grew at 21.2 percent per annum during 1982-92, is still very limited, and most long-
distance journeys are made by rail.
1.6         Transport Demand and Modal Split Trends. Although the railway's share
of taffic has declined over the last decade as the share of road traffic has grown, rail
trasport stI dominates freight and passenger traffic in China. In 1992, 57.3 percent of
freight (Table 1.4) and 45.5 percent of passenger traffic (Table 1.5) were moved by rail,
and the rest was moved by road, inland waterway, coastal shipping, pipeline, and civil
aviation. This preeminence of the railway is dne in part to the past dependence of the
Chinese economy on heavy industry and in part to the relatively long transport distances.
In the case of passenger transport, dominance of the railway is partly due to inadequate
provision of intercity bus serrices, the virtual absence of private automobiles, and the
limited use of civil aviation. The movement toward a market economy will increase
demand for market-responsive transport services, favoring a shift to road transport, but still
leaving a growing market for the reladvely low-cost rail transport system.
C. TRANSPOR REFRM
1.7        In the past decade, China's economic reforms have aimed to increase
economic efficiency through measures designed to reduce national government intervention
in the production and distribution process, reduce the Government's deficits and subsidies
to state-owned entefprises, and open the economy by expanding trade and allowing further
prtcipation of private and collective enteprises. In the tansport sector, several reforms
have been undertaken in line with these objectives. Notably, the Government has:
(a) reduced the extent of commodity price distortions by (i) decreasing the
volume of commodities produced in the State Plan; and (i:1 raising the
in-plan prices of commodities closer to market levels;
(b)   increased the tariffs charged by state transport enterprises, including
railway, highway and waterway, to levels approaching their long-run
marginal costs;
(c)   encouraged provincial governments to develop many local railways, which
now total some 4,400 route-km and intercomnnect with the natioit 1 standard-
gauge network;
(d)   allowed private individuals and collective and township enterprises to
provide passenger and freight transport services on roads and inland
waways, and encouraged provincial and national authorities to develop
trsort systems with private sector financing;



- 3 -
(e) carried out major structural reforms in the organization and management of
civil aviation and in the ports, leading to increased financial autonomy and
accountability and to less central government control, and paving the way
for introduction of competitive services;
(t)   reduced operating and capital subsidies for major inftastructure services,
through higher cost-recovery and increased reliance on user-funded capital
construction; and
(g)   encouraged the intruction of more modern technology in the transport
system, pdmarily by improving rolling stock technology in the railway
subsector; enhancing container and bulk-handling capacity in the port
subsector, improving road pavement design and geometric standards for the
ewpansion of motorization in the highway subsector; and increasing the use
of computerized data processing and plnning techniques in operations in all
modes.
In undeang many of these transport reforms, the Government has relied on the Bank
as a key source of advice, through a number of cooperative studies and programs.
D. BAN  GROUP PARTICIPATION
1.8        The Bank Group has supported initaves by the Government in the tansport
sector though loans and credits totaling $4.0 bilion for 27 projects and is now preparing
ational projects in the sector (Table 1.6 and Annex1I. These projects have included:
(a)   Ralways. Expnon of rail capacity, moderizon of locomotives and
roFlling sock, manuf ng facilities, modenization of track maintenance,
modenization of telecommunications and information systems, plus many
technical assistance and taining programs ($1,805 million in seven
projects);
0)   Highways.  Cunstruction of 1,760 kn of high-quality national and
provincial roads, upgrading of 6,430 km of rural roads, improvement of the
supervision and quality control of road construction, and studies of key
issues in road safety, pavement management, user charges and methods of
financing for road maintenance and construction ($1,149 million in nine
projects);
(c)   Ports and Waterways. Construction of terminals, jetties and berths in six
ports, dredging of navigation channels, and technical assstance and taining
for port planning, operaons management and containerization ($715 million
in eight projects);
(d)   M  ltimodal, A multimodal project for roads and inland waterways in
Jrangsu Province ($154 million); and



-4-
(e)   Urban Trnport. Two projects focused specifically on developing urban
transport in Shanghai, including road construction, traffic management and
safety, as well as overal planning and management of urban transport ($210
million in two projects). Urban transport has also been addressed through
components of several other urban sector projects.
1.9         The dialogue between the Government and the Bank Group on transport
sector operations and strategy has been strengthened through long-term strategy studies for
railways, highways and ports development, and through project preparation activities and
project-financed studies as well as sector studies carried out in collaboration with Chinese
authorities. The various stuidies have, inter alia, emphasized th: areas of multimodal
transport investment and the setting of sectoral priorities based on systemwide economic
evaluation techniques.11 A Bank Group report titled China's Railwy Strategy (No.
10592-CHA) was issued in February 1993 and a report titled Highway Development and
Management: Issues, Options and Strategies (No. 11819-CHA) is being finalized. In
addition, there are ongoing studies on the trucking industry and highway financing, and a
study of the provincial road construction industry is being included in the proposed project.
Furthermore, the Ministy of Communications (MOC) and the Bank have launched an
institutional-strengthening component for MOC in the China Reform, Institutional Support
and Preinvestment Project (Credit 2447-CHA, FY92). 1he Bank Group also conducted
a survey of the national water transport system in the mid-1980s, which has reaffirmed the
need to further exploit the large potential of the Chinese waterways. As a follow-up to the
study, the preparation of a multipurpose inland waterway project was initiated recently.
1.10        On the basis of this sector work, the Bank will broaden its involvement and
will assist the Government in the design and implementation of institutional and sectoral
reforms in other areas, including management strategy formulation, investment planning,
picing and financing, deregulation of services, operational management, and transfer of
advanced transport-related technology through training and provision of up-to-date
equipment.  Chinese authorities and the Bank Group are currently preparing a
comprehensive transport sector strategy paper that is expected to help gude sector
development in a consistent, cohesive policy framework.
1.11        The above-mentioned projects and studies are helping to spur the growth and
modernizaion of the transport system. However, much more needs to be done to bring
about the changes required to deal effectively with emerging issues. To alleviate the
tansport bottlenecks and rationalize the transport system, the Government will need to take
the following measures by the year 2000:
11  Six of the sbtuds have eplored mulmol pricing and investment issues ia more comprehenive and
economically onented analyfical framework tban was formely available to China's decisionmakers:
the Guangdog ComprebAbJve Transpot Planning Study; the Yangtze Economic Zone Transport
Study; the Coal Transport Study; the Railway Investment Study; tbe Satec Issues in Ports and
Shipping Study; and the Prie Reform Study.



-  -
(a)  increase the total level of transport investnent in relation to gross national
product (GNP), to expand capacity to meet the new traffic demands and to
clear up the backlogs causti by past underinvestment;
(b)   aim for balanced investments among the major freight transport modes of
railways, highways, and waterways in terms of optimal economic cost of
transport;
(c)   realign further the prices of freight transport services and those of the major
commodities, especially coal, to bring them closer to their real costs;
(d)  increase further the productivity of existing transport assets through
technological modernization and management reforms; and
(e)  improve and expand the multimodal transport system, both across and
within modes, with particular emphasis on more extensive use of containers.
E. THE HIGHWAY SuBsllR
The National Highway Network and its Utilizaton
1.12       The highway network totaled about 1,056,700 km in 1992 and, relative to
either population or geographic area, ranked among the sparsest in the world. About
30 percent of China's villages have no access to roads adequate for motor vehicles.
Investment in the highway subsector has been rather modest, at 21 percent of total
investment in the transport sector during the Seventh Five-Year Plan (7FYP, 1986-90).
Since the early 1980s, the highway system has carried an increasing share of taffic. The
network is, for the purposes of design and technical specifications, classified by MOC as
Expressway (access-controlled highway with divided carriageways for the exclusive use of
motorized vehicles), and Classes 1, 2, 3 and 4 for various traffic capacities in different
train. The geometric standards being used for the various road classes are shown in
Table 1.7. For administrative purposes, roads are clasfid as national, provincial,
county, village, and special purpose.
1.13       A Bank policy study on road deterioration in developing countries classified
China among the countnes with an extensive and outdated road network in need of
modernization to meet the rapid growth in road transport demand. China's road network
consists mainly of roads designed with restrictive geometric cha cs for light/
medium-weight trucks. Although China has not neglected road maintenance, its outmoded
maintenance technology would not be sufficient to armst the accelerated deterioration of
the aging road system network. In addition to upgrading the geometric pameters of the
alignments, many of the road pavements would need st ng  to cay current and
projected future taffic. China needs to mobilize domestic and extnal resources for
moderizing its road system with special emphasis placed on technology transfer,
ingstitional improvement, and development of skills. There are few absorptive capacity



- 6 -
constaints relative to achieving these objectives given China's decentralized, stable, and
financially accountable highway adminisaton structure.
1.14       The rapid growth of highway rqfic stems not only from the country's rapid
economic growth but also from other factors such as the national policy of shifting short-
haul transport from rail to road, as well as the expansion of the service secw  and rural
industries. Furthermore, with the increase in foreign tade, growth in transport demand
will be concentrated in transport corridors serving the main ports and their hinterlands and,
with the containerization of the higher-value commodities, most domestic movements of
this foreign tade are expectd to go by road. The new economic activities have genered
traffic in excess of what many of the existing facilities can accommodate. Congestion on
roads resulting from the mix of slow-moving nonmotorized and motorized traffic is a
serious problem.
1.15       The vehicfleet totaled some 6.9 million trucks, buses and cars in 1992,
with trucks comprising over 62 percent. Most trucks are four-ton capacity with outmoded
designs and low fuel efficiency. Most of the trucks and buses are gasoline powered.
These vehicles were manufacured domestically and, until recently, the production of
trucks was barely sufficient to replace the retred vehicles, let alone meet growing demand.
It was not until the early 1980s that a lare number of modern vehicles was importe and
joint venur  with foreign manucurs were sed to manufture trucks. Although
production dropped considerably in 1989 and 1990 due to the economic recesson in those
years, it has acceled since and is contributing to the creation of a sizable new fleet,
although the older vehicles predominate.
1.16       The tactor and motorcycle fleets, at 5.3 million and 6.5 million, well
exceed the size of the truck and bus fleet Both tctors and motorwcles offer a relatively
chea and basic form of transport, but add seriously to taffic congestion on rural roads
(prncipaly tractrs) and urban steet (mainly motorcycles), and are among the major
causes of road accidents.
1.17       Growth in the truck and bus fleet has been fairly steady over the last
12 years, with particularly stg gowth in small passenger vehicles. Growth in the
tra    and motorcycle fleets has increased sharply, with motorcycles showing the stronget
growth of all vehicle types (Table 1.8).
1.18       The middng lnusny consists of publicly owned trucks operated by tmuclkng
companies affiliated with local (provincial or district) highway authorities, and other trucks
opeated by fctories, coopeve organizatons and, recently, private individuals. The
rdaxation of trucldng regula   in the early 1980s allowed own-account trucks to haul
cargo for other factries and coopeafives and thus promoted compedtion between public
and own-account trucks. In fact, nonpublic trucks bave absorbed most of the growth in
taffic over the past few years (rable 1.9). However, tcking costs are extremey high,
smming in part from technlogical ft  (poor roads, outmoded trucks, relatively low
utiliztion of carrying capaity, etc.) but also from a lack of experience in flet
management, which is essential to the derelopment of modern and efficient trucldng



-7-
sevices. While market forces are expecd to play a more important role in shapg the
industry in the future, it is necessary to understand the current constraints to such
development and to identify the measures needed to allow the reshaping of the industry to
take place. In order to explore these issues more fully, a Provincial Truccing Study was
included as a component of the Henan Provincial Highway Project, and a separate study
of the Highway Trucldng Industry was part of the formal sector program for FY93.
1.19       Road saf-ty is a major issue in China's transport system. A study completed
in 1987 under the first Highway Project found the accident rate to be 100 deaths per
10,000 vehicles, one of the highest in the worla, costing the Chinese economy close to $1
billion annually. The study also identfied essential measures needed, including improved
police accident reporting; introduction of computer-based accident analysis; identfication
of accident causes and dangerous road spots; prparation of guidelines for road safety
design; improved administraive procedures; improved manpower training; and prepartion
of a road safety action plan. The responsibility for highway safety matt  was transferred
from MOC and the provincial Communications Departments to the Ministry of Public
Security and its provincial units in 1988.  This decision has created transitional
administative problems. However, these have been overcome in some of the provinces
through good cooperation between the provincial highway administration bureaus and the
concerned public security units. There has been also improvement in the accident
reporting system, as advocated in the 1987 study. In most provinces, computerzed
systems are in place. Moreover, the public security and highway bureau personnel often
work together to identify and improve road configurations at high-frequency accident
locations.
Highway Administtion, Planning and Finance
1.20       The provincial governments, through the highway bureaus of the Provincial
Transport Departments (PTDs), are responsible for adminisration of the highway net rw.
Anrangements for administraton vary among provinces; in the more decentralized ones,
the county and municipal goverments perform some adminisatve and all of the
orational functions related to the highway network. The Central Goverment's MOC
provides general policy guidance and support to the PTDs through its nationwide polic
and regulations, specfyig design and construction standards.  MOC also provides
specilized manpower for planning and design and construction equipment for major
provincial civil works, if needed. However, the PTDs are financially independent of MOC
and carry out all road planning, construction, maintenance, and adminition of roads and
watervays in the provinces. MOC is more heavily involved in certain large projects that
are considered of national importance, for which MOC shares part of the financial burden.
1.21       While the provinces manage the country's highway network, MOC is
resosible for plZandng and lnwesm   sregies for the development of die National
ighway System. A reclasification of the country's road network in 1981 led to the
designation of key interpovincial links as national highways. More recently, MOC has
been evolving a long-term strategy for the systematic upgrading of the national system in
key corridors over a 30-year planning horzon. Known as the National Trunk Highway



- 8 -
Sysem (NTHS), the system, comprising 12 major routes, would connect all the provincial
capitals and cities with populations exceeding 500,000; route selection would generally be
based on the potential for maximizing industrial production in the ccrridors selected for
the initial, minimum-length network (Map 1). Construction of 25,000 Im of high-
standard, access-controlled roads is expected to be completed by 2025. Priority for early
construction is being given to those routes already identified as congested and hindering
economic activity. By the end of 1991, some 500 km of the NTES had been built, and
a total of 4,000 km is envisaged to be completed by the end of the Eighth Five-Year Plan
(8FYP) in 1995. The provincial governments are responsible for route location,
engineering design, construction, operation and maintenance of the nationa highway
system.
1.22       The system for fiding highway constnion and maintenance is well
established throughout the country under the guidance of MOC, although there are
differences between provinces according to local needs and circumstances. The main
revenues are generated by the Road Maintenance Fee (RMF), typically levied on the
revenue of freight and passenger tansport companies and on the capacity of own-account
vehicles. The RMF is levied by the provinces, which retain the majority of the monies
after remitting a proportion to Central Government agencies. MOC also directly receives
revenues from the Added Vehicle Purchase Fee, a supplementary earmarled tax on vehicle
imports and sales, and uses these funds, which currently amount to about Y 2 billion/year,
to contribute financing for (a) the roads included in the national highway network,
providing a higher share of financing for the poorer, inland provinces and a lower share
for the richer, coastal provinces; and (b) construction or rehabilitation of rural roads
located in economically less-developed areas. According to a recent study by MOC, if
isting sources for funding both national and provincial roads are not increased by the
year 2000, they will fall 30 percent short of the amount required to develop the highway
system in line with the growth in demand. The issue is especally critical in the coastll
provinces with the fastest economic growth, where road systems are expeiencing the
highest levels of congestion and deterioration.
1.23       Over recent years, MOC has embarked on two important acdvities aimed
to improve highway pLanning and investment decisions: (a) the Road Data Bank (PDB),
which would contain all important road information at national, provincial and county
levels, with a view to provide the basis for improving the economic rationale for decisions
on expansion, rehabilitation, strengthening and maintenance of the road network. The
Highway Planning and Design Institute (HPDI) of MOC has prepared a standard format
for the dat input in the data bank and is setting guidelines for the provinces to follow so
that a uniform and fully integrated nationwide system can be establis;ied; and (b) a
Pament Management System (PMS), to improve decision-making regarding pavement
design and maintenance based on economically appropriate standards, including opinal
tming. The Highway Scientific Research Instttute (HSRI) of MOC has taken the lead in
this work, investigating alternative PMkSs available in most developed countries and some
developing countries and adapting themn to Chinese needs.



-9 -
1.24       In addition to its role in planning and financing, MOC serves an important
function as coordinator for the coastruction of priority sections of the national highway
network; in early 1990, MOC established a Technical Coordinating Unit (TCU) to
maintain close contact with the provinces executing projects financed by the Bank Group.
The TCU's main focus has been to provide guidance to the project executing agencies and
to coordinate staff training and the development of the RDB and PMS at the provincial
level.
1.25       In swwnary, despite government efforts to improve the highw'y system and
its udlization and administration, much more is needed to modernize the system to
effectively meet the increasing demands placed on road transport, by improving both
physical capacity and management of the sector. The key areas, as reflected in the recent
Highway Development and Management Paper (para. 1.9), on which the government
should focus attention for improvement through institutional reforms and training are:
(a)  the role of MOC, to be made consistent with the rapid administrative
decentralization taking place in the management and financing of highway
development and maintenance, and for MOC to be strengthened to carry out
its reformed mandate, particularly in matters such as finance, standards for
planning, design and generation of the NTHS (including safety and
facilitation), and training;
(b)   highway planning and investment strategies, to better identify short- and
long-term development priorities;
(c)   feasibility studies, for preparation utilizing modern highway analytical
techniques, based on data and relationships reflecting conditions on China's
highways;
(d) highway maintenance, to be modernized through improved planning and
new technology in order to optimize network performance;
(e)   cost recovery and financing, to provide the necessary resources to meet
increasing financing needs for construction, maintenance and operations, and
to encourage efficient use of the highway system;
(f)   operations of the road user services industry, both freight and passengers,
to reduce costs and improve quality of services, through promotion of
competition, modern technology and better management; and
(g)  the road construction industry,  .nprove management, understanding and
application of contract condition.; to modernize equipment; and to clarify
client, contactor and supervision relationships.

The Bank's Experience and Strategy In Highways
1.26       Of the 10 Bank-financed highway projects,2/ only the first Highway
Poject has been completed (Table 1.10). The project, which was designed to construct
and improve 7 segments of the national and 59 segments of the provincial/county road
networks in nine provinces, achieved its original objectives and clearly demonstrated the
bene.its accruing from improved accessibility to communities deprived of adequate
transport infrastructure in economically less-developed regions. Economic activity in the
area of influence of most project roads surged and income levels increased noticeably.
Furthermore, the Bank's involvement facilitated the acceptance of competition in
contracting for road works in preference to force account operations, even for smaller civil
works contracts, and of tne merits of supervision of construcdon by specially assigned
units.
1.27       Highways together with port projects constitute the Bank's first major
nvolvement with large civil works in China. As could be expected, introduction of
international competitive bidding (ICB) has been difficult, particulrly as each project
involves a new implementing agency. Despite these difficulties, which have led to most
civil work components in highways projects falling behind appraisal schedule, much
progress has been achieved and the concept of competitive bidding is now generally
accepted. The projects have allowed the Bank to gain an understanding of the weaknesses
in the capacity and experience of the local contracting industry and, similarly, of the
executmg agencies in contract administration. These lessons are being used in the
prepation of new  projects addressing these issues, particularly strengthening
prequalification of contractors to avoid selection of unqualified contractors and
strengthening arrangements for construction supervision. The Bank's involvement has
assisted the line ministry and the provinces' transport departments to develop greater
awareness of the need for systematic planning through the development of the RDB and
PMS; the Bank has also helped in the transfer of technology through the supply of
equipment for road research and road maintenance and through broad-based training
prograris covering highway technology and advanced management practices.
1.28       The Bank's serwgy forfifure highway endng for the medium term will
continue to accord high priority to the development of the NTHS and the upgrading of the
provincial road networks. This strategy envisages close collaboration with MOC and
assisting it to adapt to the evolving needs of the Chinese economy (para. 1.25). Highway
projects would routinely address the aspects of environmental protection, resetdement of
individuals affected and road safety improvements, and would also focus on improving
methodologies for investment analysis and the tansfer of advanced technology for design
and implementation. As a key feature of these projects, the Bank would provide assistance
for selected institutional development at central and local levels and sectoral reforms aimed
at modenizing the highway system and its management. The issues expected to be
addressed in the proposed project and in forthcoming projects include the following:

(b)   promotion of adequate road freight and passenger services, including

2. FUJIAN PROVINCE                SPORT SECTOR
A. GEoGRAC Am ECONOMIC SmF=G
2.1        Fujian Province, with an area of about 121,000 Inm2, is situated along the
southeast coast of China. Eighty percent of the land area is classified as mountainous,
scattred through the middle and northern portions of the Province. An additional
10 percent of the land is classified as hilly, and the remaining 10 percent flat or rolling.
The flat and rolling areas are located along the Province's 3,300 kn eastern coastline. The
rivers flow from the western mountains to the eastern sea. This ge graphical configuration
presents a natural barrier to the flow of communication between Fujian and neighboring
provinces and retards the development of north-south and east-west transport linkages
within the Province.
2.2        Notwithstanding its geographical constraints, Fujian Province has flourished.
The population of the Province has grown steadily, increasing from 27 million in 1985 to
nearly 31 million in 1992, with a projected population of about 37 million by the year
2010. In nominal terms, the GNP more than tripled between 1985 and 1992, growing at
an average annual rate of over 20 percent, while the Gross Output Value of Agriculture
and Industry (GOVAI) grew at 24 percent annually. The Province projects annual GNP
growth of 12 percent well into the naxt century. Comparative economic data for Fujian
Province during the period are as follows:
Population (thousand)  27,131      31,760
GNP (Y million)        19,197      70,520
GOVAI (Y million)      27,281     120,418
2.3        Proximity to Taiwan (China) and Hong Kong, coupled with preferential
government policy, has led to the establishment of successful special economic zones at
Xiamen and Fuzhou. The Province has approved a large number of foreign investment
projects, comprising nearly 10,650 joint ventures, individual-ownership enterprises, and
cooperative enterprises, with contracted foreign investment of about $17.0 billion. Of this
number, 4,398 enterprises have begun operations. Facing the Taiwan Straight on the east,
Hong Kong, Macao and Southeast Asia on the south, and bordering the rapidly developing
provinces of Zhejiang and Guangdong, Fujian is strategically located for accelerated
economic development.
2.4        There ar six main cities (Fuzhou, Xiamen, Quanzhou, Zhangzhou, Putian,
and Sanming) and three prefectua (district) administrive offices, as well as 54 counties,
10 county-level towns and 17 municipal districts in the Province. The capital city,



- 13 -
Fuzhou, and the other five major cities together comprise over 70 percent of the provincial
population.
B. TRANsPORT NErWORI
2.5        Fujian Province has a well-developed communications and transport setor
consisting of roads, rail, inland waterways, ports and civil aviation. The total length of
the surtace transport network was approximately 46,200 kan in 1992. Roadways composed
91 percent of the total network, while navigable waterways and rail represented 7 percent
and 2 percent of the total, respectively. A considerable additional length, approximately
10,400 kIn, consists of waterways that are nonnavigable.
2.6        The about 8,100 km main arterial road network connects all major cities,
ports and industrial zones in the Province. The five national routes passing through the
Province (National Route Nos. 104, 316, 319, 205, and 324) have six interprovincial exits.
The existing road between Quanzhou and Xiamen (part of National Route No. 324) links
the Province with Beijing in the north and Guangzhou in the south. In Fujian, as in
China's other provinces, the road network is divided into four classes of roads ranging
from high-class roads with bituminous or concrete pavement (Class 1) to unpaved roads
(Class 4), with an additional category for unclassified roads. In 1992, a major proportion
of the provincial network was composed of Class 4 or unclassified roads, representing
66 percent and 25 percent of total road network length, respectively. Class 1 and Class
2 roads together accounted for 1 percent of the total road network, while Class 3 roads
constituted 8 percent of the total. The composition of the Province's road network in the
period between 1980 and 1992 is shown in Table 2.1. The administrative clification
of the road system in 1992 was as follows:

2.7        At present there are two arte  railways that link the Province with the
nationwide network. The Yintang-Xlamen Railway provides a link to Jiangxi Province,
and connects to seven locations within the Province. The second major railway, the
Waiyang-Fuzhou Railway, operates between 22 stations witiin the Province, with a major
connection point at Nanping. Plans are under way for construction of rail lines from
Hungfun to Nanping and from Janping to Xiamen, to be completed with the next five
years. While the rail network remains small, rail transport provides important links with
remote mountainous areas of the Province and with other regions of China. In this regard,
the railway is used for distribution of key commodities, such as coal, coke, petroleum,
steel, metl and nonmetal ores, construction materials, fertiz, grain, cotton, and salt.
Passenger routes to points outside the province, such as Beijing, Nanchang, Guangzhou,



- 14 -
Shanghai, and Nanjing are well-traveled, with bus transportation generally preferred fo.
shorter trips.
2.8         Waterway transportation in Fujian Province includes an inland waterway
network as well as coastal shipping. There are five main navigable inland waterways in
the Province used for transporting a variety of goods, particularly between coastal areas
and the mo;ntainous northwestern region of the Province. However, the majority of
inland waterways remain shallow and nonnavigable. Coastal shipping provides vital access
to overseas markets for import and export of a wide range of industrial, agricultural, and
light manufactured goods. Major waterway lines exist between Fujian and points such as
Hong Kong, Japan, Singapore, Malaysia, and Sri Lanka. The Xiamen Port is capable of
handling ships with ca.4ity over 30,000 tons. Fujian's coastal ports reached a total
throughput capacity of 17 million tons of freight capacity and 660,000 passenger capacity
in 1992. The major ports at Quanzhou and Xiamen have freight handling capacity of 2 and
7 million tons, respectively. The Xiamen port also has a passenger capacity of 383,000
persons. Development efforts in the waterways subsector include both inland waterway
and port expansion. The Shiaco power station and dam construction on the Ming River
would increase water depth to 500 m, improving access for large ships.  Further
development of the Xiamen Port includes doubling the number of berths at the port (from
4 to 8), expanding the container facilities and improving road access to the port.
2.9         Air transport in the Province, as in the rest of China, has expanded
substantially in recent years. Airports are located in thme cities: Fuzhou, Xiamen, and
Wuyishan. The major points of departure are Fuzhou and Xiamen airports, each offering
flights to over 30 domestic and international points, for a combined total of over 150
flights weekly. There are presently two airlines based in the Province, the Xiamen Airline
Corporation LUnited, with domestic and international operations, and Fujian Lianhang
Airlines, a domestic carrier.
C. TAFIC CHARACrhRICS AND GROWTH
2.10        Traffic, as measured by the volume of goods and number of passengers
tansported, grew significantly on all modes between 1985 and 1992. For freight traffic,
the fastest annual growth was evidenced by air transport, which grew at 162 percent per
year, followed by water (32 percent), road (24 percent), and rail (19 percent). Passenger
traffic also grew most rapidly in air transport, at 275 percent per year, followed by road
(26 percent), water (19 percent), and rail (15 percent). Despite the vast growth of air
transport, this mode represented a negligible proportion of total freight or passenger traffic
volume in 1992, less than 1 percent in each category. Road transport dominated both
freight and passenger taffic volumes in 1992, capturing 81 percent and 93 percent of each
category, respectively (Table 2.2).
2.11        Turnover of surface transport in the Province increased substantially during
the 1980-92 period, with an annual growth of about 10 percent for both freight and
passenger traffic. In both categories, road traffic grew more quckly than traffic on other
modes of surface transport, resulting in an increased share of total traffic on roads. The
proportion of passenger traffic represented by road transport increased from 61 percent in



- 15 -
1980 to 76 percent in 1992. The proportion of freight traffic transported on roads
inreased from 23 percent in 1980 to 30 percent in 1992 (Table 2.3).
D. TtANspoRT ADMNISRATON
2.12        Transport for each mode is administered by various authorities at provincial
and national levels. Rail lines form part of the national network under the authority of the
central Ministry of Railways; however, the construction of new rail lines is presently
managed locally, by prwvincial transport authorities. Air transport is administered by the
Fujian Provincial Civil Aviation Administration. Highway and waterway transport are
administered by the Fujian Provincial Transport Department (FPTD). The organization
of highway administration and its different administative functions ace described in
Annex 2.
E. TI  VEHcLE Fuwr
2.13        The total vehicle fleet has grown rapidly since the early 1980s. In 1992,
total vehicles numbered 773,8.9, reflecting compound annual growth of 15 percent since
1980. Between the years 1985 and 1992, the total vehicle fleet grew by 277 percent. The
most rapid growth during the 1980-92 period occurred in the motorcycle and small truck
categories, which both grew at 40 percent per year. During the same penod, the number
of medium and small passenger vehicles (with capacity of fewer than 20 passengers) grew
at 18 percent per year, and large passenger vehicles grew at 11 percent per year. In terms
of the proportion of total vehicles in the Province, tractors and large/medium truck;
dominated the fleet in 1980, representing 62 percent and 23 percent of total vehicles,
resectively. By 1992, however, motorcycles, small and medium passenger vehicles, and
small trucks were also well-represented in the total vehicle fleet. The composition of total
vehicles in 1992 was motorcycles (49 percent), tractors (30 percent), large and medium
trucks (9 percent), small and medium passenger vehicles (6 percent), small trucks
(3 percent), and large passenger vehicles (2 percent) (Table 2.4).
F. T lE TRUCKING TLwusY
2.14        Road freight services have expnded significantly since 1980. Between 1980
and 1992, the total volume of freight carried by trucks grew by about 13 percent annually,
while freight turnover grew by 10 percent annually, and the average distance traveled grew
by 3 percent annually. The trucldng industry has exhibited the same trend as observed in
other areas of China, such that an increasing proportton of total road freight services is
being provided by own-account operators (enterprises, collectives and private individuals)
while the share of truckdng services provided by public transport companies affiliated with
PTD has declined. Between 1980 and 1992, the share of total freight volume carried by
public tansport companies declined from 29 to 7 percent; the corresponding share of total
freight urnover handled by public transport companies declined from 40 to 11 percent.
The average distance traveled by both public tranrt and own-account operators increased
by 3 percent annually between 1980 and 1992. However, the average distance traveled by
public transport operators remained 60 percent greater than that of own-account operators
thoughout the period (Table 2.5).

G. ROAD SAIm
2.15        Road traffic accidents, as measured relative to the number of registered
vehicles in the Province, have declined since 1987. The number of road traffic accidents
per 10,000 vehicles declined by 17 percent each year, while fatalities and injuries declined
by 12 percent and 20 percent per year, respectively, between 1987 and 1992. Both the
number and the severity of accidents on the existing Fnizhou-Xiamen Highway increased
between 1980 and 1992. While the total number of accidents increased by 6 percent per
year, the number of fatalities increased by 10 percent per year and the number of injuries
increased by 4 percent per year. These data are indicative of the sharp accident growth
along certain trunk roads in China, where the combination of mixed traffic and high
volume of motorized vehicles increases the risk and severity of accidents.  The
construction of access-ontrolled highways for I.e exclusive use of motorized traffic
nonnally leads to a reduction in accidents, as a large proportion of motorized traffic
becomes separated from slow-moving traffic, such as tractors and bicycles, and the
incidence of vehicle conflicts is greatly reduced through the provision of interchanges.

3. THE PROJECT
A. Pnojwr FORMULATION AD PREIARATnON
3.1        In the early 1980s, the Central Government of China (GOC) initiated a
comprehensive road development program which was implemented during 1986-90, the
period of the 7FYP. The 8FYP provides for continuation of this program at an accelerated
rate, in recognition of the need to expand and improve the national and provincial road
networks to meet increasing traffic demand in corrdors with a high level of economic
activity, and to foster economic growth in the less-developed regions of the country. In
1989, GOC requested the Bank's continued assistance for the subsector, submitting to the
Bank a list of highway proposals at the end of that year.
3.2         As a follow-up, a Bank mission, in conjunction with State Planning
Commission (SPC) and MOC representatives, visited several provinces during February
and March 1990 to make a preliminary assessment of nine highway proposals, identifying
those highway projects eligible for possible financing during the 8FYP. From this
preselection process it was concluded that the proposal of the Fujian Province to construct
a new high-standard highway between Xiamen and Quanzhou, as Phase I of the longer-
term objective of providing a high-standard highway between Zhangzhou and Fuzhou, was
a viable proposition warranting consideration for Bank financing. In tenns of priorty, it
was agreed with GOC that the processing of the proposed highway in Fujian Province
should follow preparation of the Zhejiang, Guangdong, and Henan Provincial Highway
projects.
3.3         The proposed project was prepared under the direction of a Leading Group
set up by the Provincial Government for the construction of the Zhangzhou-Xiamen-Fuzhou
Expressway by FPTD and the Fujian Provincial Expressway Construction Directorate
Offie (FPECDO), with the guidance of SPC, MOF and MOC. The Bank's project
identification mission visited Fujian Province in November 1991, with subsequent Bank
nissions working closely with the Provincial and Centrl governments to define the scope
of the project. The main components of the proposed project are the construction of a new
highway between Xiamen and Quanzhou, a road network improvement program for the
upgrading and paving of provincial and county roads located primarily in the less-
developed northern and northwestern regions of the Province (Map 2) and a study of the
provincial road construction industry.
B. ftOJECr OBJECrPE  AN  RATIONAE FOR BAN  INVOLVEMN
3.4         The proposed project has the following specific objectives:



- 19 -
(a)   to support the continued development of road infrastructure in Fujian
Province to facilitate mobiiity and increase the efficiency of road transport
in the interest of stimulating economic activity;
(b)   to develop an action plan for the strengthening of the provincial road
construction industry;
(c)  to assist in the development of institutional capabilities for transport
planning and investment prioritization; and
(d)  to facilitate the transfer of highway construction and maintenance technology
through staff training and the provision of modem laboratory and road
maintenance equipment.
3.5         The proposed project is consistent with the Bank's Country Assistance
Strategy, presented to the Board in August 1993, which is further elaborated in para. 1.28
regarding the objectives for highway financing in China. The new Xiamen-Quanzhou
highway to be constructed under the proposed project is an important link in the coastal,
interprovincial highway network. Construction of the proposed highway will relieve
eisting traffic bottlenecks along the most congested corridor in the Province, particularly
along the most heavily trafficked section between Xiamen and Quanzhou. The highway
will also provide direct links to the Xiamen Special Economic Zone and the Port of
Xiamen, complementing earlier Bank assistance by improving access to the port's newly
expanded cargo-handling facilities.
3.6         The project's support for a Road Network Improvement Program (RNIP)
would also help extend the benefits of economic growth to relatively disadvantaged areas
or areas where the substandard road system now constrains development. In addition, the
project will provide the Province with road condition survey equipment and computer
hardware and software to facilitate the introduction of methodologies for economically
based investment decisions. Equipment for road laboratories and road research to support
technological improvements in road construction and road maintenance will also be
provided.
3.7         The study of the provincial road construction industry aims to identify
appropriate measures for increasing the capacity, efficiency and financial viability of local
road construction enterprises.l/ The study would focus on the enterprise reform
initiatives of Fujian Province as they impact the transformation of state-owned enterprises.
The funds included in the project from the loan proceeds would be supplemented by a
portion of the technical assistance (TA) grant obtained from the Japanese Government
earlier this year for a highway construction industry study. The remaining portion of the
gant funds would be used to carry out a nationwide survey of the highway construction
industry, which will be designed primarily to identify the overall profile of the industry.
j11  Tbe ptsent stawus of the provincial road construction industry is descrbd in Section B of Annex 2.



- 20 -
Such "aseline information would then be used in the formulation of a broad strategy for
the futue development of the industry.
3.8        During preparation of the project, the personnel of local institutions have
been exposed to up-to-date project methodologies and practices, such as economic
evaluation, engineering design, environmental assessment and procurement, as a usual
complement to the Bank's involvement. In addition to such experience gained by the
institutions, the project provides for staff training opportunities in a variety of highway-
related topics as an avenue for technology transfer.
C. PowEr SCOPE
3.9        The scope of the project includes:
(a)   construction of an 81.4 km section of the Xianen-Quanzhou four-lane
divided, access-controlled highway, a Class 1 standard-access highway to
Xiamen (6.2 kIn) and a Class 2 standard access road to Shedian (6.3 km);
(b) tWe supply and installation of electrical, electronic, and mechanical
equipment for tolling, traffic monitoring, telecommunications and lighting
for the highway, and ventilation, lighting and safety devices for tunnels;
(c)  improvement of three provincial road sections (58.3 kIn) and paving of four
county road sections (202.7 km);
(d)   construction supervision of the Xiamen-Quanzhou Highway and the
provincial and county road improvement works;
(e)  the purchase of equipment for:
(i)  opeation and maintenance of the Xiamen-Quanzhou Highway;
(ii)  road laboratories;
(iii) the development and application of a Road Data Bank and a
Pavement Management System; and
(f)   a study of the provincial road construction industry to identify appropriate
measures for increasing the capacity, efficiency and financial viability of the
provincial road construction enterprises; and
(g)   staff training programs covering a broad spectrum of highway-related topics
such as planning, design, construction, traffic operations and maintenance.
3.10        Construction of the Xiamen-Quanzhou Highway. The existing road
between Xiamen and Quanzhou (102 km) forms part of National Route No. 324, which is



- 21 -
a section of the so-called Coastal Route of China connecting northeastemn Heilongjiang
Province with Hainan Island located in the most southern part of the country. The Coastal
Route is one of the 12 strategic routes that have been identified for systematic upgrading
to provide high-standard, access-controlled highways for the exclusive use of motorized
traffic. Route No. 324 links up with. the Beijing-Fuzhou highway (Route No. 104), the
Fuzhou-Lanhou highway (Route No. 316), and the Xiamen-Chengdu highway (Route No.
319). Five of the six main cities in Fuzhou Province (Zhangzhou, Xiamen, Qiianzhou,
Putian and Fuzhou), all located along the existing Zhangzhou-Xiamen-Fuzhou route. are
the most economically developed and densely populated in the Province, representing about
62 percent of the Province's population of about 31 million. There has been a strong
linkge between economic development and traffic growth. Since 1985, the GOVAI of the
corridor's five cities has increased at an annual rate of about 18 percent, while the ann-ial
increase in traffic over the same period was about 17 percent. The pavement of the
existing road has been widened to 9-12 m (Class 2 standard), extending the road's design
capacity to about 10,000 motorized vpd. However, the traffic, which is now in the range
of 14,000 to 16,000 motorized vpd, is contributing to the high number of road accidents,
congestion and slow travel speeds, particularly through the numerous townships loc.ted
along the route. The widening of the existing road to provide the additional road capacity
required would not be feasible because of extensive roadside development, particularly
along a 30 km section south of Quanzhou.   Furthermore, segregating the rapidly
increasing long-distance motorized traffic from slow-moving, nonmotonzed traffic can only
be achieved if a parallel, high-strAndard, access-controlled highway is built.
3.11       The feasibilty study and engineenng design for the highway was undertaken
by the Fujian Provincial Transport Planning and Design Institute (FPTPDI). Several
alternatives were investigated for the location of the various sections of the highway,
particularly for the end-sections near Xiamen and Quanzhou and at the location of major
bridges and tunnels. During the assessment of these alternatives, in addition to technical
considerations, the potential adverse impact on the environment and resettlement has also
been taken into consideration. Foreign consultants, financed from the proceeds of
Consultant Trust Funds (CTF), have provided assistance to the Province in the preparation
of design, technical specifications and bidding documents. The highway traverses
geologically difficult terrain requiring extensive bridging and tunnehng works.
Furthermore, the highway alignment traverses about 17 kan of soft, highly compressible
soil sections over which special treatment of the subsoil will be required to ensure the
stability and orderly settlement of embankments. A 2.7 km long embankment section of
the bighway is being constructed to test alternative solutions for stabilizing embankments
and mitigating the extent of embankment settlements.
3.12        The proposed highway would be constructed to expressway standards as
stipuated in the national geometric standards for highways (Table 1.7). Connections to
the existing road network to and from the new highway would be provided at six
interchnges (M    3). The highway project would also include four tunnels, five major
bridges, 23 medium-sized bridges, 43 small bridges, 275 culverts, 41 grade-separation
bridges, 12 pedestrian overpasses and 130 road underpasses. The highway would be
operated as a toll facility (para. 4.12). In addition to major civil works ,md the provision



- 22 -
of roadside facilities, such as buildings for administation, road side services and
maintenance depots, the project includes the supply and insallation of electrical, electonic
and mechanical devi-as required for toll collecdon, traffic monitoring, telecommunications,
tmnel ventilation and safety systems and lighting. The engineeng design and technical
specifications for the highway have been reviewed by foreign consultants engaged under
CTF and were found to be satisfactory.
3.13       A 2.7 km section of the highway is under corstruction as part of a research
program on soft-soil treatment methodologies for highway embanlunent construction. The
rearch is being conducted by FPTPDI, and the construction of the section is being
financed from the Province's own resources. Similarly, the construction of a Cla 1
standard-access highway to Xiamen (6.2 kim), and service facilities along the highway
would be financed from the Province's own resources.
3.14       Road Network lmprovement Program (NP). About 80 percent of
Fujian Province is hilly and mountainous, and the economic development of several
regions, particularly those located in the northern and northwesten part of the Province,
has been impeded by inadequate road infrastructure. Most of the roads in these regions
have poor geometric standards and unpaved road surfaces that make road transport slow
and costly. The Province intends to upgrade the condition of the rural arterial road
network gradually. As part of the program under the 8PYP, the Province proposed to
include in the project the upgrading of thre road sections (58.3 km) and the paving of four
road sections (202.7 kim). These roads are geographically widespread in recognition by
the Province hat the Bank's involvement in project financing would promote improved
project preparation practices to regional tsport units, foster the transfer of technology
and imprve the quality of project imptation on a broader base. The rationale for
the selection of the particular road sections was detennined by respective regional
piorities.
3.15       The engineening design and economic evaluation have been prpred for all
sven road sections included in the RNIP. The technical standards and economic
justification for the proposed upgading and paving works were reviewed during project
appraisal and were found to be satisfactory. The description of the influence area of the
individual roads, the engineering considerations, the details of the economic evaluatior. and
the organizational anangements for the progrm's implementation are presented i. 
Annex .
3.16       Supervion of Construction.  Although independent supeison of
construction quality and progress is gaining general acceptance in China, local staff still
have limited experience in the supervison of major highway contracts, particularly those
awarded under Interatonal Competitive Bidding (ICB). Therefore, the construction of
the Xiamen-Quanzhou Highway would be supervd by a joint local/foreign supervision
team. The Joint Supervision Team (JSI) will be responsible for ensunng that construction
wors for the highway are carried out in full compliance with the technical spocifications,
and that strict quality control are applied on site. The local supervision peronnel would
be provided primarily through the services of the FujianI Highway Consulting Enginee



- 23 -
Inc. (FHCEI), a recently established enterprise. Additional supervision personnel required
will be seconded to the JST from prefecturl and county transport agencies. The foreign
supervising engineers would be integrated into the organization with operational
reponsibilities. Furthermore, the foreign consultants would help establish procedures at
all levels of supervision process, including on-site inspection and laboratory testing. The
foreign consultant's contract would also provide for a comprehensive program to train local
staff in construction supervision. Since the responsibility of JST will extend to the
enforcement of the environmental protection measures to be taken by the contractors as
stipulated in the civil works contracts, environmental protection aspects would also be
included in the training program for supervision staff. An understanding was reached
during project appraisal on the terms of reference (TOR) for the services of JST and the
organizational arrangements for the supervision. About 125 staff-months of foreign
consultant services are envisaged, including that for staff training. Local staff service
requirements are estimated at about 4,300 staff-months.
3.17        Provincial Road Construction Industry Study (PRCIS). The objective
of the study is to identify the external and internal constraints that limit the provincial road
construction industry's operational efficiency and to develop a set of recommendations and
a plan of action to increase the industry's capacity, efficiency and financial viability.
3.18       The study would determine the most likely scenario for supply and demand
relationships of road works, talkng into account the Province's medium-term road
development and road maintenance programs and the present capacity of the stateowned
and collectively owned road construction enterprises, together with the in-house capacity
of the provincial government's direct labor resources for road construction and
maintenance. The study would also analyze the prevailing constraints on the enterprses
in terms of equipment, manpower and financial assets. The level of technology being
applied in road construction and maintenance, and the level of skills manifested in
opeational and financial management would also be assessed with a view to identify
effective corrective measures.
3.19        A comprehensive review would be made of the prevailing policy, legal and
regulatory framework in which the enterpnses operate. In line with nationwide
government policy, the Ptovince has prepared directives for the transformation of state-
owned enterprises to market-oriented operations. The likely impact of such directives on
the reorientation of the road construction enterprses would be closely examined, including
contactng practices of the government entities, tax burdens, credit restrictions or other
regulations or business practices that may hinder the healtiy development prospects of the
industry.
3.20       The study would be carried out by a Study Team, comprised of foreign and
local experts. The team would be guided by a Steering Committee and a Technical
Coordination Group. The funds for the study from the loan proceeds would be
supplemented by a portion of a TA grant obtained from the Japanese Government in early
1993. The remaining portion of the Japanese grant funds would be used to carry out a
nationwide survey of the highway construction industry that would identify the overall



- 24 -
profile of the highway industry in China. Such baseline information would be used in the
formulation of a broad strategy for the future development of the industry. A brief
summary about the features of the highway construction industry in China, and in Fujian
Province, and outline TORs for the pilot study in Fujian and the nationwide highway
construction industry survey are presented in AnngA 4. Assurances were obtained at
negotiations that Fujian Province (FP) will (a) undertke and complete a study of the
provincial road construction industry in accordance with TOR, by June 30, 1995,
satisfactory to the Bank, (b) establish a Steering Committee and a Technical Coordination
Group to provide guidance to the study group and review the findings and
recommnendations of the study; and (c) take appropriate steps to implement the study's
recomunendations, including the formulation of an action plan, taking into account the
views of the Bank.
3.21        Staff Training. In addition to the training of local staff in construction
supervision, a series of training programs would be conducted, covering various aspects
of highway engineering. To allow for larger group participation, a portion of the training
program included in the project would be organized locally with the participation of foreign
and local experts. As with ongoing Bank-financed provincial highway projects, highway-
related training programs, organized abroad for provincial staff, would be coordinated and
administered by MOC in the interest of efficiency and economy. The Traning Division
of FPTD would be involved in the implementation of the program. The trainees would
be selected from the staff of FPECDO, FPID, FPHIAB and FPTPDI according to the
relevance of their academic backgrounds, experience and expected future assignments.
The training areas of major interest for Fujian provincial personnel were identfied during
project preparation as outlined in Table 3.1. The program covers topics directly relevant
to the main focus of the highway sector orientation of the Province and the components of
the project. Assurances were obtained at negotiations dtt FP will cary out the steff
training program in accordance with TOR satiactory to the Bank.
3.22       Road Data Bank (RDB). An RDB to manage highway information has
been developed in MOC for countrywide application. A standard format for the data to
be coUected has been designed and a set of guidelines has been prepared to introduce the
system to the provinces in a phased manner. Initially, the Bank provided assistance to
MOC to help with the development of the RDB under the first Highway Project (Loan
2539-CHA/Credit 1594-CHA). This assistance has been followed up under ongoing Bank-
financed highway projects in the provinces of Sichuan, Shaanxi, Jiangxi, Shandong,
Jiangsu, Zhejiang, Guangdong, and Henan. It has been used primarily to finance the
purchase of instruments, equipment, and computer hardware and software to enable
systematic collection and processing of input data for the system. Under this project, the
same type of equipment would be provided to assist the setting up of the RDB system in
Fujian Province.
3.23        Pavement System (PM).  The Province has a well-
administered road maintenance system with well-defined objectives, road condition
assessment and budgeting procedures and a financial monitoring system (Section B of
Annex 2). However, increases in road traffic and the use of heavier vehicles are taking



- 25 -
their toll on the relatively light-structured pavements in the Province. Maintenance funds
have normally been widely spread to cover most of the network's readily identifiable short-
term  maintenance needs.  However, FPHAB intends to adopt more advanced
methodologies for developing longer-term road maintenance strategies and for guiding
investment decisions with closer-to-optimal resource use. HSRI has developed a PMS that
has been introduced and is being applied in several provinces. In Shandong, Shaanxi,
Jiangxi, Sichuan, Jiangsu, Zhejiang, Guangdong and Henan provinces, the implementation
of the PMS has received assistance under Bank-financed highway projects. The project
would provide assistance for the PMS development in Fujian Province by financing
equipment and instruments for the pavement condition survey and data processing through
computer applications.
3.24       An understanding has been reached during project appraisal that, in line with
MOC's policy objectives to introduce the RDB and PMS programs nationwide, FPHAB
would set up and adequately staff a PMS/RDB Unit before the end of 1994 and, with
assistance to be provided by MOC, would develop both the RDB and PMS programs in
the Province.
3.25       Equipment. Equipment will be provided under the project for: (a) the
operation and maintenance of the Xiamen-Quanzhou Highway; (b) road laboratories and
field survey units of FPECDO and FPHAB; and (c) road condition measuring instruments
for the RDB and PMS programs. The need for equipment under item (a) arises from the
eventual task of maintaining and operating the new highway. Equpment under item (b)
would be required to replenish/upgrade road laboratory equipment stocks, particularly soil,
bitumen and asphalt pavement testing equipment that previously have not been widely used
in China. Equipment under item (c) would be used in connection with the introduction of
the RDB and PMS programs in the Province. The items and estimated cost of the
equipment are presented in Table 3.2.
D. ENVIRONMENTAL ASSSME
3.26       Xiamen-Quanzhou Highway. An Environmental Assessment (EA) for the
Xiamen-Quanzhou Highway was completed by the Fujian Provincial Environmental
Protection Research Institute (FPEPRI) during 1991/92 with the assistance of FPECDO.
It has been ascertained that the highway will not affect ecologically sensitive areas and, if
appropriate actions are taken, the construction and the operation of the highway should
have no adverse impact on the environment. Based on the finding of the EA, and
subsequent to discussions between FP and the Bank, an Environmental Action Plan (EAP)
was prepared in early 1993 and submitted to the Bank in mid-May 1993. The EAP
specifies the measures and organizational and monitoring arrangements required to mitigate
potential adverse effects on the environment during the construction and the opetation of
the highway. The main measures relate to social disruption, air quality, noise control,
water management, control of trffic, operation of quarries, and waste disposal. The
engineering design and technical specifications for the highway incorporate the appropriate
measures required to be taken for environmental protection. Administrative arrangements
have been agreed upon to ensure the enforcement of such measures during the construction



- 26 -
and the operation of the highway. The EAP was reviewed during project apaisal and
was found to be satisfactory. A summary of the EA and EAP is presented in Annex S.
Copies of the EA and EAP reports are available in the Project Pile (&qc&.- Assurances
were obtained from FP at negotiations that the environmental protection measures
stipulated in the EAP with respect to the Xiamen-Quanzhou Highway would be camed out
In a manner satiactory to the Bank.
3.27       Road Network Improvement Program. The works under the RNIP will
be directed to the improvement of three roads and paving of four gravel-surfaced roads.
No major risks of adverse impact on the environment were identified in the course of the
review of the proposed scope of works. Nevertheless, the potential minor adverse effects
on the environment during the implementation of the three Group A roads have been
assessed and an EAP has been prepared for these roads. The EAP was reviewed during
project appraisal and was ftound to be satisfactory.
3.28        Paving of the existing Group B roads would present very limited risk of
adverse environmental impact. Nevertheless, it was agreed during project appraisal that,
as a precautonary measure, contractors of these subprojects will be instucted to follow
a Code of Environmental Practice for Road Works. It will be the responsibility of the
constuction supervision and local representatives of the Fujian Provincial Environmental
Protection Bureau (FPEPB) to ensure that the contractors adhere to such practice.
Assurances were obtained from FP at negotiaions that the envronmental protecdon
measures stipulated in the EAPs with respect to the RNIP would be carried out in a manner
satifactory to the Bank.
E. LAND AcQuismoN ADREITLEMNT
3.29        Xiunen-Quanzhou Highway. The Land Administration Law of China and
the Fujian Provincial Land Administration Regulations provide the legal basis for planning
and implementing land acquisition and resetding persons affected by the construction of
the highway. Land acquisition and resetdement are well-regulated in China; procedures
and compensation requirements generally conform to te Bank's policies on involuntary
rsettlement. A Resettlement Action Plan (RAP) for the Xiamen-Quanzhou Highway,
including an outline for monitonng of the implementation of the plan, prepared by
FPECDO has been reviewed by Bank staff and was found to be satisfactory. The
approximately 15,500 persons who are expected to be affected by the construction of the
highway would be adequately compensated and resetded. The resettlement process will
be administered by the respective local govemments at city/county, township, and village
levels, in accordance with the terms of agreements signed between FPECDO and the local
govemments involved. The objective is to keep the resetders in productive employment
either rough assigning new land area or providing employment in close proximity to the
presently held land or habitat. According to the RAP, all land acquisition and resetlement
actions are scheduled to be completed by the end of March 1994. A summary of the RAP
for the Xiamen-Quanzhou Highway is presented in Annex 6. A copy of the RAP is
available in the Project File (Annex 9). Assurances were obtainedfrom FP at negotations



- 27 -
dw aU land acusition and resettlement activties isvth respect to the Xiamen-Quanzhou
Highway would be compkted according to the RAP in a manner satiftory to the Bank.
3.30       Road Network Improvement Progrim. RAPs have been prepared for each
of the Group A roads. The resettlement procedures for these subprojects are the same as
those for the main highway, although the levels of compensation differ slightly from those
applicable for the highway. According to the RAPs, about 700 individuals will be resetted
and compensated under the plan. The RAPs were reviewed during project appraisal and
were found to be satisfactory. A summary of the RAPs for the Group A roads of the
RNIP is presented in Annex . Since the paving of Group B roads would not involve a
change in alignment, the need for resettlement will not arise. Assurances were obtained
firom PP at negotiatons that the RAPs for roads under the RNIP woud be carned out in
a manner sairfactory to the Bank.
3.31        The implementation of the RAPs for both the Xiamen-Quanzhou Highway
and the Group A roads included in the RNIP would be monitored through routine project
supervision and quartly progress reporting (pam. 3.52). The implementation and the
socioeconomic impact of resettlement on the population would also be evaluated according
to a resetlement monitoring plan outlined in the RAP for the Xiamen-Quanzhou Highway,
a summary of which is presented in &nex . Assurances were obtained from FP at
negotiations ht, the monitoring of the effectiveness and socioeconomic impact of the
resettkme for both the Xamen-Quanzhou Highway and the roads of the RNIP will be
camed out according to monitonng plms in a mamer satactory to the Bank.
