H. IMPEMNTATION
3.36       The implementation of the project would be the responsibility of FP.
PPECDO would be the executing agency for implementing the Xamen-Quanzhou
Highway, under the direction of a Leading Group for the implementation of the
Zhangzhou-Xiamen-Fuzhou Expressway, headed by the Vice-Governor of the Province.
Since the new highway is a segment of the national highway network, MOC would also
provide general supeision and guidance for execution of the project. The highway would
be constructed under four unit-priced civil works contacts to be awarded following ICB
procedures. There would be a separate ICB contract for the supply and erection of E&M
devices for tolling, traffic monitoring, telecommunications, lighting, and tunnel venftlation
and safety. Construction of the highway is scheduled to commence in the second half of
March 1994, and be completed in about 48 months. The prequalification of contractors
was completed in August 1993, and bidding documents to prequalified contractors are
scheduled to be issued in September 1993. The installation of electrical, electronic and
mechanical systems for the operation of the highway is scheduled to start in mid-1997 and
be completed by the end of 1998. The construction of service faities along the highway
would commence in early 1996, and it would take about two-and-a-half years to complete.
3.37        The organional and staffing a gements for construction supervion
of the Xiamen-Quanzhou Highway have been prepared and were agreed durng project



- 30 -
appraisal. Construction will be supervised by JST, headed by a Chief Supervision
Engineer. A Resident Engineer will be located on sitz ior each of the contracts. Resident
engineers will report to the Senior Supervision Engineer's Office through the Office of the
Engineer's Representative. About 100 technical personnel will be permanently engaged
in supervision. The selection process of foreign supervision consultants is scheduled to be
completed by November 1993. The consultants would be mobilized in January 1994 to
begin training of local personnel in construction supervision and help setting up the
supervision organization. FHCET will be the local participant in the JST together with
personnel to be seconded to JST from prefectural and county transport units. FHCEI,
established in March 1993, was awarded a Grade A license by MOC for carrying out
highway consultancy and supervision services. Registered with the Administrative Bureau
of Industry and Commerce, FHCEI is a state-owned enterprise, and is qualified as an
independent business entity. The staff of FHCEI, as former employees of the Fujian
Provincial Highway Administration Bureau (FPHAB), have been extensively involved in
the management and supervision of highway construction works.
3.38       EPHAB will be the executing agency for the RNIP. A special unit has been
set up in FPHAB to administer the program, coordinate the various activities with al
concerned agencies, ensure the consistency and the quality of design, and meet overall
implementation management, monitoring and progress reporting requirements (AnnexO).
Additional laboratory equipment needs of the units involved have been included in the list
of equipment to be provided under the project. PPTPDI, in cooperation with the relevant
design institutes of municipalities and prefectures, have completed the preparation of final
engineering designs as well as draft bidding documents for the roads included in the RNIP.
Construction of the subprojects will be supervised by FHCEI, with the participation of
transport bureau staff at the county and municipal levels, under the overall supervision of
FPHAB. FPHAB was the principal agency for implementing the Fujian component of the
first Bank-financed Highway Project, and its performance was satisfactory.  The
construction of the seven roads of the RNIP is scheduled to commence in mid-December
1993 under retroactive financing and be completed in 18 to 24 months. Assurances were
obtained from FP at negotiations that FPHAB would t stablish and maintain a RNIP Unit
within FPHAB with qualified and experenced personnel to provide overall management
and supervision for RNIP, and that the transport adminustrations of the respectve
prefecturail/municipal and county governments would be adequately staffed with qualf/ed
and experiencedpersonnel andprovide other necessary resourcesfor the execution of road
works under the RNIP.
3.39       The responsibility for procurement of equipment would be divided between
the two executing agencies. FPECDO would be responsible for the procurement of
equipment related to the Xiamen-Quanzhou Highway component, such as equipment for
the operation and maintenance of the highway. FPHAB would administer the procurement
of equipment for the road laboratories and RDB and PMS. Equipment for operation and
maintenance of the highway would be procured during 1997/98. Laboratory equipment
would be procured during 1994, and equipment for RDB and PMS during 1995/96.

3.42        Procurement arrangements for the project have taken into account the
experience gained from previous Bank-financed highway projects. The documentation for



- 32 -
procurement, which covers the prequalification of contractorm, ICB and Local Competitive
Bidding (LCB) for civil works, ICB for gods, and consultant services contracts, has been
standardized for Chinese conditions in cooperation between the Chinese Government and
the Bank. Model documents will be used for all relevant procurement processes under the
project. In particular, prequalification criteria for civil works under ICB procedures have
been tightened, and are based on performance requirements specifically related to each of
the contracts. Under previous highway projects, the Bank accepted that minor and
scattered civil works, similar to those included in the RNIP, would be implemented by
force account because of the scarcity of construction industry units in remote areas of the
country. However, during recent years a sufficient number of construction enterprises
units have been established, which are capable of undertakdng road improvement works
following competitive bidding processes. Since the capacity of the road construction
industry is still limited, providing further opportunity to construction enterprises units to
participate in LCB under this project would enhance the technical developm.ent and growth
of the road construction industry.
3.43       The four civil work contracts for the construction of the Xiamen-Quanzhou
Highway, with contract amounts ranging from an estimated $60 million to $82 million,
would be awarded following ICB procedures, at an estimated total contract value of $413
mllion. Contractors, according to their bidding capacity, determined during the evaluation
of the prequalification submissions, could submit bids for one or more contracts. ICB
procedures would also be followed for the supply and installation of electrical, electronic
and mechanical equipment required for the toll facilities, traffic monitoring,
telecommunication services, tunmel ventilation and safety and lighting systems. The
construction of a 2.7 km soft-soil testing section of the highway, a Class I access highway
to Xiamen (6.2 kIn), and service facilities for the highway, comprising buildings for
administration, toll stations, maintenance depots, service and rest areas, and assocted
minor civil works along the highway, would be financed by FP from its own resources,
at an estimated amount of $25 million.
3.44        Contracts for the improvement of ffiree roads and the paving of four
additional roads under the RNIP component of the project would be awarded individually
following LCB procedures. These roads are widely dispersed in the northern and
northeastern region of the Province and are not suitable for packaging. The contract
amounts range from $1.5 million to $14.0 million, totaling about $40 million. The
Province, in the interest of utilizing favorable weather conditions in December 1993 and
in the early part of the new year, prior to the onset of the rainy season, has requested the
Bank's concurrence to advance procurement and retroactive financing for the RNIP. The
Bank's agreement to FP's request is recommended subject to FP's compliance with
procurement requirements in conformity with the Bank's Guidelines on Procurement. The
amount of retroactive financing required is estimated at $2.0 million.
3.45       Equipment for the operation and maintenance of the Xiamen-Quanzhou
highway would be procured following ICB procedures at an estimated contract amount of
about $2.9 million. All other items or groups of items covering equipment for
laboratories, field survey, and equipment for RDB and PMS programs would be procured



- 33 -
on the basis of a comparison of price quotations solicited from at least three suppliers
eligible under the Bank Guidelines, in accordance with procedures acceptable to the Bank.
3.46       Under ICB, qualifying domestic contractors and manufacturers would be
eligible for a margin of preference in the comparison of bids of 7.5 percent for civil works
and 15 percent for goods, or the prevailing customs duties, whichever is lower. Each civil
works contract exceeding $3.0 million and each contract for the procurement of goods
exceeding $200,000 would be subject to prior review and agreement to procurement
documentation by the Bank. This would represent about 97 percent of the estimated
amount of all contracts under the project.
3.47       The need for foreign consulting expertse has been identified for the
supervision of construction, staff training, and carrying out the provincial road construction
industry study. Since the consultants are expected to be mobilized in February 1994 to
commence the training of local staff to be assigned to construction supervision, provision
for rebtoactive financing at an estimatid amount of $700,000 is considered appropriate as
a precautionary measure in case of possible delay in signing the loan agreement. All
foreign consultants would be employed under terms and conditions acceptable to the Bank
in accordance with Bank Guidelines on the Use of Consultants. Local expertise would also
be extensively used wherever appropriate.
J. DIURSEMES
3.48       Disbursements of the Loan would cover:
(a)   32 percent of total expenditures for highway civil works;
0b)   25 percent of total expenditures for the RNIP;
(c)   100 percent of foreign and 75 percent of local expenditures for the supply
and installation of E&M equipment for the highway and tunnels;
(d)   100 percent of foreign expendiures for directly imported equipment or
100 percent of local expenditures (ex-factory) for locally manufactured
equipment and 75 percent of local expenditures for other items procured
locally; and
(e)   100 percent of expenditures for consulting services and staff training.
3.49       To facilitate disbursement, a Special Account would be opened with an
initial deposit in US dollars of $9.0 million, the estimated average expenditure for a four-
month period. The account would be opened in a bank acceptable to the Bank, and would
be managed by FP. Applications for replenishment of the Special Account would be
submitted monthly or whenever the Special Account is drawn down to 50 percent of its
initial deposit, whichever comes first. A schedule of estimated disbursements is provided
in Table 3.4. This schedule talkes into consideration the disbursement profile for transport



- 34 -
projects in China and the potential risk of delays in project implementation due to the
difficult terrain, including subsoil conditions and tunneling requirements.
3.50       Disbursements would be made against priced contracts for the civil works
and delivery and installation of the equipment. Interim certification of civil works
completed and costed at unit rates provided in the contracts will be done by the JST, and
payments will be authorized by FPECDO and FPHAB. Disbursements for staff training
in China or abroad will be made against the actual costs of travel, subsistence, and tuition
or training fees. Reimbursement for expenditures made on contracts for goods and civil
works not exceeding $200,000 and $3,000,000 equivalent, respectively, would be made
on the basis of statements of expenditures (SOEs). Documentation supporting SOEs would
be retained by the implementing agencies and would be made available for review by Bank
supervision missions. Disbursements, in an aggregate amount not exceeding the equivalent
of $2,700,000, may be made for expenditures incurred after October 31, 1993 on project
components indicated in paras. 3.44 and 3.47, under retroactive financing.

4. ECONOMC EVALUATION
4.1         The economic analysis focuses on the Xiamen-Quanzhou Highway and the
RNIP components of the project, which account for about 97 percent of the project's base
cost. The economic analyses were initially prepared by FPTPDI for both components
during 1991/92, and were reviewed and updated by Bank staff in consultation with
FPTPDI personnel during project preparation.
A. XAME-QUANZOU HIGHWAY
4.2         The Existing Corridor. The proposed Xiamen-Quanzhou Highway would
be the first phase of a high-standard, access-controlled highway the Province intends to
construct in the corridor formed by the Province's major and most rapidly growing cities:
Zhangzhou, Xiamen, Quanzhou, Putian and Fuzhou. The economic activity of the
Province is concentrated in these five coastal cities. While the cities comprise only
23 percent of the total land area and 41 percent of the total length of the road network in
the Province, together they produce about 69 percent of GOVAI, and account for
62 percent of the population of the Province. Land transportation in the corridor is almost
exclusively highways-oriented since there is no railway line in the corridor. Air transport
service within the corridor is available only between Xiamen and Fuzhou.
4.3        Xiamen, one of the first four coastal cities designated as a special economic
zone, was opened to foreigners in the early 1980s. GOVAI of Xiamen constitutes about
12.5 percent of the provincial total, and the city plays an important role in the economic
development of the region. The city is located at the mouth of the Jiulong River, the
second-largest river and port of the Province in terms of traffic throughput. However, the
total land area, road length, and population of Xiamen account only for 1.2, 2.1 and
3.6 prcent of the provincial totals, respectively.
4.4        Roads provide the only means of land transport between Xiamen and
Quanzhou, the second-largest city and fourth-largest port of the Province, located at the
mouth of the ring River. The rapid economic development of both cities has generated an
accelerated growth in traffic and a noticeable increase in traffic congestion, particularly
along built-up sections of the existing road network. This is manifest in the decrease in
average corridor travel speeds observed in recent years. The expected furither steady
increase in road traffic has prompted the Province to pursue the construction of the first
major highway to meet traffic demand along th important corridor.
4.5         Traic on the Existing Road. The existing road between Xiamen and
Quanzhou, built in 193C, was upgraded to Class 2 road standard in 1990, with a design
capacity of about 10,000 motorized vpd, and with an average design speed for motorized



- 36 -
vehicles of about 80 kan/hour. During 1980-92, the average traffic growth rate for
motorized vehicles came to 9.2 percent per annum; during the 1985-92 period, the annual
growth rate averaged 16.7 percent. Road tolls were introduced on the existing road in
1991. In 1992, the level of traffic reached about 15,000 motorized vpd. The effect of
mixed traffic, including slow-moving, nonmotorized traffic, combined with the overall
increase in traffic volumes has resulted in: (a) a decrease in average travel speeds for
motorized traffic to a 40-45 km/hr range along most sections of the existing road during
daytime hours, and (b) an increase in the number of traffic accidents.
4.6        An Origin and Destination (O-D) survey was conducted in 1990 and a
supplemental O-D survey in early 1993. The surveys covered 19 zones, and were
comprehensive enough to provide data regarding travel characteristics, particularly with
respect to the likely future traffic diversion from the existing road to the proposed
highway. The O-D surveys also confirmed the consistency of the level of traffic estimated
through periodic traffic counts and that the composition of the trffic is predominantly
passenger transport-oriented, with small buses representing 39 percent of traffic. Medium-
size trucks represented only 24 percent of the traffic.
4.7        TraI ic Projection. The economy in the corridor, as reflected by GOVAI,
has been projected by the Province to grow at 13.2 percent per annum to 1997, dropping
to 10.9 percent between 1997 and 2005, and to 7 percent between 2005 and 2015. By
companson, the average traffic projection used in the economic evaluation-6 percent
growth until 1998, 7 percent between 1998 and 2007, and 6 percent between 2007 and
2017-is considered to be conservative. The relatively lower growth rate assumed until
the opening of the new highway for tffc takes into consideration the effect of the
capacity constraints of the existing road. Based on the data derived from the O-D surveys,
it was estimated that about 70 percent of the motorized traffic would be diverted from the
existing road to the new highway. The rationale for anticipating such a relatively high
level of diversion is that: (a) the new highway intersects the existing road at several
points, and through the interchanges to be provided, a larger portion of the medium-
distance traffic would benefit from the use of the new highway through travel distance
savings (17.6 km) and travel time savings; (b) the Province intends to continue charging
tolls on the existing road even after the opening of the new highway for traffic, which is
also expected to encourage diversion of traffic to the new highway (para. 4.12); and (c) as
indicated by t-- 4inding of the O-D, about 30 percent of the large truck and truck-trailer
traffic is through-.Affic with origins and destnations beyond Xiamen and Quanzhou. The
level of generated taffic, resulting from the impact of improved traffic conditions to be
provided by the new highway, has been estimated at 15 percent of the normal traffic. The
projected traffic in the corridor, and its estimated split between the new highway and the
existing road, is presented in Table 4.1.
4.8         Economic Analysis. The economic evaluation has been done separately for
three homogenous sections of the highway: (a) Xifu (Quanzhou)-Chidian section,
(b) Chidian-Kangdian section, and (c) Kangdian-Guanglintou (Xiamen) section. In the
economic analyses two scenarios were assumed: operation of the highway with tolls and
without tolls. The economic costs were derived from financial costs by deducting taxes,



- 37 -
duties and interest payments. The financial cost, economic cost and adjustment factors for
shadow pricing for the three sections of the highway is presented in Table 4.2. The
analysis of shadow price conversion factors is shown in Table 4.3.
4.9        The highway is scheduled to be constructed in four years and opened for
traffic in 1998. The vehicle operating cost and time savings are quite substantial because
of the shorter travel distance of 17.6 km using the new highway. In addition to the cost
of construction, the cost of periodic maintenance (pavement overlays) was taken into
account in the tenth and eleventh years after opening the highway for traffic. The annual
costs of expressway maintenance and toll operations, for the with-toll scenario, have also
been included in the economic evaluation.
4.10        Benefits were estimated to accrue primarily from savings in vehicle operating
costs and travel time. Additional quantifiable benefits arise from generated traffic, reduced
congestion on the existing road, and savings in road maintenance and accident costs.
Average vehicle operating costs by vehicle class, pavement type and condition, and terrain
are shown in Table 4.4. These costs also reflect variation in vehicle speeds for the
different types of vehicles traveling on the existing road and on the new highway. The
cost of reduction in traffic accidents is based on the assumption that the rate of reduction
will be one accident per kilometer per year at a cost of Y 10,000 per accident.
4.11       The economic evaluation summary and sensitivity analysis for the Highway
is shown in Table 4.5. The economic internal rate of return (EIRR) for the highway as
a whole is estimated at 24.3 percent with the tolling scenario that, in the most extreme case
of the sensitivity analysis, assuming a two-year delay in the completion of the highway,
is reduced to 19.1 percent. Without tolling of the highway, the base EIRR is 27.0 pernt.
The EIRRs for the Xifu (Quanzhou)-Chidian, Chidian-Kangdian and Kangdian-
Guanglingtou (Xiamen) sections of the highway are 28.4, 28.8 and 19.9 percent,
resectively, in the with-tolling scenario (Tables 4.6-4.8). The lowest level of EIRR of
15.8 percent was found to be on the Kangdian-Guanglingtou (Xiamen) section of the
highway under the with-tolling scenario, assuming a two-year delay in completion of the
highway.
4.12        ToMling. The Province intends to operate the new highway as a toll facility
conistent with its design. Tolls will be collected at the end points and at the interchanges,
representing a closed system for toll collection. Tolls are being collected along the existing
Xiamen-Quanzhou road, and the Province intends to maintain the present system as a
means of cost recovery earnarked for road works, even after the new highway becomes
operational. This practice is expected to encourage a somewhat higher diversion of traffic
to the new highway than would normally tale place. To ensure that the toll rates would
be appropriately structured, and that toll levels would not lead to a substantal reduction
in the benefits of highway investment, the rationale for setting the level of tolling will be
detemnined based on the findings of a study to be undertaken by the Province. Assuranes
were obtainedfrom FP at negotiations that by July 31, 1997 FP wig complete, and make
ailable to the Bank for comments, a study on the rationale for detemdning toll rates,
according to the Outl. , Temnw of Reference in Annex 8.



- 38 -
B. RoAD NErwoRK ImpRoVEMENT PROGRAM
4.13         The RNIP comprises seven subprojects, divided into two groups: Group A
includes three roads to be upgraded (58.3 kIn), and Group B consists of four gravel roads
that would be surfaced with asphalt penetration macadam (202.7 km).
4.14         The volume of traffic on the three Group A roads in 1992 reached an
average of 2,660, 1,450 and 1,250 vpd, respectively, while on the four Group B roads
traffic volumes were at a fairly uniform level with only a small variation between 430 and
560 vpd. Historical traffic growth rates indicated a similar overall pattem for the RNIP
roads over the past seven years, varying between 4.8 and 6.5 percent. It was assumed that
during the first five years following improvement of the road, traffic will grow somewhat
faster than indicated by the historical trends, with some tapering off during the following
10 years.
4.15         The main quantifiable benefits associated with RNEP include: (a) reduced
vehicle operating costs on the upgraded roads due to improved geometry, improved
suraces, and reduced travel distances; (b) reduced travel time and congestion costs due to
road upgrading and elimination of bottlenecks; and (c) reduced road maintenance costs.
4.16         Economic Analysis. The economic analysis considered projected traffic
growth, subject to capacity constraints on the road. Additional traffic was assumed to be
generated by the improved road conditions at a rate of 10 percent of the normal taffic.
Vehicle operating costs were estimated in two steps: first, free-flow operating costs were
estimated; then the values were modified to reflect the degree of traffic congestion. Road
maintenance costs were assumed to comprise a portion of fixed cost per ldlometer per
year, plus variable cost measured as a linear function of daily traffic volumes. As
summarzed below, the estimated EIRR for individual subprojects varies from 17.9 to
44.0 percent, with an overall average value of 31.1 percent. Details of the economic
evaluation are presented in Annex 3.

C. NAIB BENEnS Am BENmCAR
4.17        The main quantifiable benefits associated with development of the highway
and the seven rural arterial roads include savings in vehicle operating costs, travel time,
and road maintenance costs, and savings through reduction of congestion and traffic
accidents. The analyses of economic cost and benefits yield an EIRR of about 24 percent
for the Xiamen-Quanzhou Highway and about 31 percent in aggregate for the road sections
included in the RNIP. The overall weighted average EIRR is 25 percent. Benefits from
the projects would accrue to road users, including the operators of freight and passenger
transport services, enterprises and farmers, in the form of reduced vehicle operating costs.
Moreover, many road users are own-account operators and should capture the cost savings
directy. PassenZers would also benefit from shorter travel time and improved riding
comfort. The Province would benefit from reduced cost of road maintenance, and a broad
segment of the community would benefit from the reduction in traffic accidents. B)enefits,
particularly from the RNIP, would accrue from improved access to remote areas and
greater mobility for social activities, fostenng accelerated economic growth. The primary
beneficiares of the RNIP are the residents of those towns and villages that are served by
the existing poor-quality roads that are to be upgraded. While the project's institutional
benefits are not quantifiable, they are nonetheless impe iant. The involvement of local
personnel in project preparation, requiring particularly X .gorous treatment of economic and
evironmental assessment, design details, technical specifications and construction
practces, will provide benefits far beyond the implementation period of the proposed
project through the experience gained by the personnel involved. Similar institutional
benefits would be derived from the RNIP through the exposure of transport bureau staff
to more advanced technological and administrative processes. The specific attention given
to the road construction industry in Fujian Province to encourage transition to market-
onented operation is also an initiative with good potential for long-term benefits.
D. PRojEr RIS
4.18        Based on Bank experience with ongoing highway projects, the main risk is
implementation delay, particularly for the highway component. Because of the difficult
subsoil conditions along some sections of the highway alignment, the relatively high
number of umels and bridges to be constructed and the extensive rock excavation
involved, there are major risks of delay in the timely completion of this highway. Since
Fujian Province is a first-time borrower for a major Bank-financed highway project, such
lack of experience adds a furher element of risk to the timely implementation of the
project. However, project preparation has benefited substantially from the Bank's previous
expeience with similar highway projects in China, as well as from the active involvement
of MOC in project preption, the participation of foreign experts in the design of the
highway and the preparation of bidding documents and technical specifications, the
stringent cnteria used for the prequalification of contractors and the carefil formulation
of supervision arrangements to ensure regular monitoring of implementation and effective
quality control.



- 40 -
S. AGREEWMS REACHED AND RECOMNDATION
5.1        Agreement was reached with the Central Government at negotiations that it
would onlend the proceeds of the loan to FP under arrangements presented to the Bank at
negotiadons (para. 3.35).
5.2        During negotiations, assurances were obtained from FP that:
(a)   FP will (i) undertake and, by June 30, 1995, complete a study of the
provincial road construction industry in accordance with terms of reference
satisfactory to the Bank; (ii) establish a Steering Committee and a Technical
Coordination Group to provide guidance to the study group and review the
findings and recommendations of the study; and (iii) take appropriate steps
to implement the study's recommendations, including the formulation of an
acdon plan, taking into account the views of the Bank (para. 3.20);
(b) the staff training program would be carried out in accordance with TOR
satisfactory to the Bank (para. 3.21);
(c)  the environmental protection measures stipulated in the EAPs with respect
to the Xiamen-Quanzhou Highway and the RNIP would be carried out in a
manner satisfactory to the Bank (paras. 3.26-3.28);
(d)   all land acquisition and resettement activities with respect to the Xiamen-
Quanzhou Highway and the RNIP would be completed according to the
kAPs in a manner satisfactory to the Bank (paras. 3.29-3.30);
(e)  the monitoring of the effectiveness and socioeconomic impact of
resetdement for both the Xiamen-Quanzhou Highway and the roads of the
RNIP would be carried out according to monitoring plans in a manner
satisfactory to the Bank (para. 3.31);
(f)   FPHAB would establish and maintain an RNIP Unit within FPHAB with
qualified and experienced personnel to provide overall management and
supervision for the RNIP, and that the transport administrations of the
respective prefectural/municipal and county governments would be
adequately staffed with quaified and experenced personnel and would
provide other necessary resources for the execution of road works under the
RNIP (para. 3.38);

(h)   by July 31, 1997, FP will complete and make available to the Bank a study

- -                            AX1
BANK GROUP PARTICIPATION IN
THE T SPORT SECTOR
1.          Through August 1993, the Bank Group had loaned about $4.0 billion to
China under 27 projects for the transport sector (Table 1.6) and there are a number of new
transport sector projects under preparation.
2.          The railway subsector has received $1,805 million in Bank Group funding:
five loans to the national railway system (Loan 2394-CHA; Loan 2540-CHA; Loan
2678-CHA/Credit 1680-CHA; Loan 2968-CHA; Loan 3406-CHA; and Loan 3581-CHA)
and one loan to che local railway system (Loan 3060-CHA!Credit 2014-CHA). These
projects have helped the government expand capacity on key routes and modernim the
technology for manufacturing electric locomotives, passenger coaches, and signaling
equipment. The local railway project is financing the construction of a line in Inner
Mongolia.  The most recent project is assisting MOR with railway technology
moderization; development of systemwide plans, planning tools and costing studies; and
adding capacity where it is urgently needed. The audit of the First Railway Project noted
that physical components were well executed and that macropolicy adjustments, especially
in the area of tariff reforms, and investment prioritization were likely to have the highest
impact; these conclusions are being considered in the currently proposed railway prQjet.
3.          The highway subsector has received $1,149 million in Bank GrouP financing
under nine projects (Loan 2539-CHA/Credit 1594-CHA; Loan 2811-CHA/ Credit 1792-
CHA; Loan 2951-CHA/Credit 1917-CHA; Loan 2952-CHA; Credit 1984-CHA; Loan
3073-CHA/Credit 2025-CHA; Loan 3471-CHA; Loan 3530-CHA; and Loan 3531-CHA).
These projects provide for the construction of high-standard national highways totaling
about 1,630 kin, and the expansion and improvement of some 5,140 km of lower-class
provincial and county roads and major bridges. When completed, some of these roads will
help to provide vital missing links in the national highway network; others will hell to ease
severe congestion in parts of the network or provide access to remote areas. The projects
are also designed to provide foreign technical assistance and training of Chinese personnel
in areas such as supervision and quality control of road construction, design, and planning.
Finally, the projects support studies on key issues of highway development such as road
safety, pavement management, user charges, and methods of financing the expansion,
improvement, and rehabilitation of the road network. The first Highway Project (Loan
2539-CHA/Credit 1594-CHA) ii completed; a Project Completiorn Report was issued in
May 1992.
4.          The port and waterways subsector has received $715 million of Bank Group
financing under eight projects (Lan 2207-CHA, 2689-CHA, 2877-WHA/Credit 1845-
CHA, 2907-CHA/Credit 1975-CHA, 3006-CHA, 3007-CHA, Credit 2391-CHA, and Loan

3552-CHA). Most of the loan funds are being used in Shanghai, Tianjin, Dalian,
Huangpu, Ningbo, and Yiamcn for construction of terminals, jetties, and berths for
handling bulk commodities, general cargo, and containers, as well as the dredging of
navigation channels. Funds have also been used for training, technical assistance, and
various studies such as port planning, operations management, and containerization. The
audit report of the first port project found the project successfully completed, on time and

incorporated in the Bank's most recent port project, in Shanghai, and in the discussion of
procurement procedures for other transport projects under preparation.

roads, the project also includes the first Bank financing in China for the rehabilitation of
waterways, a 17 km a section of the Grand Canal, and associated works, such as a
shiplock and a bridge.
6.         The Bank Group has financed two specific urban transport project totaling
$210 million (Credit 2296-CHA and Loan 3652-CHA). The Shanghai Metropolitan
Transport Project was designed to increase capacity and reduce congestion in the transport
network; and improve the planning, programming, finance and management of the urban
transport system in Shanghai. The Second Shanghai Metropolitan Transport Project would
further address operational and economic efficiency of the urban transport system and
strengthen public sector management capacity. While these two projects singularly
addressed urban transport, several other Bank-financed projects in the urban sector also
include transport components.

HIGHEWAY MAAGEMEN
A. HIGHWAY ADMINRAToN
1.         The Fujian Provincial Transport Department (FPMD) administers most of
the highway, port and waterway transport-related matters in the Province through its
various departnents.  Notable exceptions are the administration of the Xamen and
Quanzhou ports (pan. 2) and the new Zhangzhou-Xiamen-Fuzhou Highway (pam. 4).
ID reports direcdy to the Fujian Provincial Government and acts as an arm of the
provincial government in transport planning, budgeting, and financing. Activities of FPID
adhere to general transport policy and technical guidelines of MOC.
2.          While PPTD is responsible for all planning, construction, and maintenance
of the provincial road network, prefectures, municipalities, counties and towns/villages
have some autonomy in planning and investing in small-scale road construction. The
Department is also responsible for port and waterway administration including shipping,
except Xiamen and Quanzhou ports, two major ports in the Province that were recently
placed under the direct responsibility of their municipal governments.
3.          The Fujian Provincial Highway Administration Bureau (FPHAB), one of the
divisions of FPTD, is responsible for highway matters and receives professional and
technical guidance from the Engineering Adminisrton Department of MOC. With a
headquarters staff of about 160, and total staff of 12,000, the bureau's main function is the
constuction and maintenance of roads through local admisations set up in the various
municipalities, prefectres, districts and counties throughout the Province. The nine
Tansport Bureau offices at the municipality and prefecture level carry out construction and
maintenance of the national and provincial highways using experienced, fu-time staff
within their maintenance units. Under the municipalities and prefectures, 67 offices at
district and county levels have responsibility for the maintenance of lower-level roads.
Some townships, enterprises, and mines also take part in the maintenance of roads of their
particular concern, termed 'special roads."
4.          The Fujian Provincial Expressway Construction Directorate Office
(FPECDO) has been set up recently as an enterprise for administering the preparation and
construction of the Zhangzhou-Xiamen-Fuzhou Highway under the direction of a
governmental Leading Group headed by a Provincial Vice Govemor. FPfD is represented
in the Group by its Deputy Director. At full strength FPECDO will have 60 staff.
5.          The             strucures of FPTD, PPECDO and FPHAB are presented
in Charts 1 and 2.



- 80-                         ANNEX 2
6.         Planning and Budgeting. Planning is carried out by FPTD on an annual
bais, within the framework of five-year investment plans. The planning process for
annual investment in road construction begins in the early part of the year, followed by
discussions and provincial approval during July/August. Approval by MOC, when
required, is typically obtained by November. The steps involved in processing and
approval of road investments varies according to scope and amount of investment for the
proposed project. For projects over Y 100 million, a feasibility analysis, including
economic evaluation, is submitted by FPHAB to MOC for approval. Projects under Y 100
million may be approved by the Province, with a copy of documents submitted to MOC
for subsidy consideration. MOC subsidies (grants) are available for construction of
national and provincial roads, but not for maintenance. Investment in local, county, and
township roads (generally in the Y 1 to 2 million range) requires neither provincial nor
MOC approval, though copies of documentation would typically be submitted to relevant
authorities to apply for subsidies.
7.         Like most other provincial transport departments in China, FPID follows
a balanced budget policy for managing highway investments as well as recurring
expenditures. The proposed highway expenditures are normally balanced against the
revenues earmarked for highway maintenance and development. Since 1985, the Province
has also maintained a Transport Fund, comprising monies earmarked for investment. In
1993, the responsibility for financial planning and budgeting for the road subsector was
devolved from FPHAB to the prefecture/municipality level of the highway bureau
organization. As a restlt, highway bureau units at each level undergo an annual planning
and budgeting process, with review and approval by, and transfer of funds through,
FPHAB. There are three main categories of maintenance for the purposes of budgeting:
engineering (including construction arid maintenance activities), administration, and other
expenditures. While the financial planning process involves a balanced budget approach,
funding gaps may be identified, which would be met by transfers from FPHAB.
8.         Road user charges, in particular Road Maintenance Fees (RMF), are
collected by transport bureaus at various levels and redistributed to meet the funding
requirements of the entire Province. RMF funds ;,- allocated to prefectures and
municipalities on a pro-rata basis, according to each unit's actual collection of the total
provincial RMF each year. The municipalities of Fuzhou and Xiamen, which generate a
large portion of the Province's RMF, in amounts well beyond their own budgetary
requirements, retain 60 percent of their share of the RMF. Other prefectures and
municipalities retain 80 percent of their share of the RMF. The remaining portion of RMF
funds are retained by FPHAB in a general pool at the provincial level, used to meet
funding gaps of lower-level highway bureau units during the year. Road maintenance
opeations constitute the major use of RMF revenues: 95 percent of funds are allocated
for maintenance, with the remaining 5 percent for construction.
9.         The provincial highway budget for Fujian amounted to Y 694 million in
1992 (representing 21 percent annual growth since 1985). Of this amount, 48 percent was
collected from road maintenance fees, 15 percent was contributed by MOC, 12 percent was
raised through tolls, and 10 percent was provided by the Province. The most significant



- 81-                         AMM  2
growth among revenue sources has been in tolls collected, which increased 22-fold between
1985 and 1992 (i.e., at an annual rate of 55 percent) (Appendix 1). The major iteims uf
expenditure during the 1985-92 period were maintenance and construction. In 1992,
maintenance accounted for 48 percent of expenditures, while construction accounted for
38 percent of expenditures. Other components of total expenditure included rehabilitation
(11 percent) and administration (3 percent). The proportion of expenditures reprsented
by construction has increased significantly, from 17 percent in 1985 to 38 percent in 1992.
The proportion of expenditure on maintenance declined from 68 percent of total
expenditures in 1985 to 48 percent in 1992.
10.        Flnancing. As discussed above, funding for highway construction and
maintenance is derived from a variety of provincial and national sources. However, the
large majority of funding is derived from provincial sources. The RMF is the major
source of funding, representing 48 percent of total highway funding in 1992. The fee is
collected from motor vehicle operators throughout the Province. Of the gross RMF
collected, about 82 percent is retained for application to the Province's highway budget.
An additional 15 percent is allocated to the Ministry of Finance Energy and Transport
Fund and 3 percent is transferred to the provincial Public Security Bureau (primarily for
highway patrol and road safety operations). The gross RMF collected by Fujian Province
increased by over 15 percent annually between 1985 and 1992, from Y 148 million in 1985
to Y 405 million in 1992. The net RMF retained by the Province also increased by
15 percent annually over the period, from Y 122 million in 1985 to Y 334 million in 1992.
11.        The RMF is collected from atl vehicle operators, with rates applied based
on the vehicle weight: for passenger vehicles, a 4-passenger capacity would be charged
at a 1-ton rate. RMF rates are determined by the FPID and approved by the State
Council. Current rates are as follows:
Vbicle categgy         Monthly rate (Yuan)
Trucks                    140 per ton
Buses                     160 per ton
Tractors                   40 per ton
Passenger cars            160 per ton
Motorcycles                 2 per unit
Among the vehicle categories, trucks contribute the largest portion of RMF, currently over
50 percent of total fees collected. The collection ratio for the RMF is 71 percent, after
accounting for exemptions, evasions, and varying numbers of vehicles operating from
month to month. Certain categories of vehicles are exempted from the RMF, such as
buses with local routes (routes of less than 30 km) and vehicles of public administrations
(such as government, hospitals, and fire departments).
12.        In 1992, the majority of RMF revenues were collected from private (or
"own account") vehicle operators, with significant amounts also collected from transport
enterprises. Social enterprises and institutions contributed 49 percent of total RMP



- 82 -                        ANNEX2
revenues in 1992, followed by private transport enterprises (25 percent) and publicly
owned transport enterprises (17 percent). The remainder was collected from government
and social organizations and schools (3 percent), organizations in the timber industry
(1 percent), public transport entities (1 percent), and other organiztions (3 percent).
13.         Tolls represent an increasingly important source of funding for the highway
subsector in the Province. Toll revenues are earmarked for highway expenditures, broadly
defined. Tolls may also be introduced to finance a specific road venture: in such a case,
toll revenue would be held in a reserve fund for repayment of underlying debt of the toll
road corporation and administration of toll operations. Revenue in excess of these
obligations could be applied to a vaiety of other highway sector uses; howewcr, 'he
Province generally restricts the use of toll revenues to the constructicn of new roads.
14.         During 1993, the Province introduced a number of preferential policies to
support highway investment. The policies are designed to genert funds that would be
earmarked for highway investment. A portion of the new funding could, in the medium
term, be applied to investment in other priority subsectors, such as telecommunications or
ports. A significant amount of the new fiunds would be collected through road user
charges. Toll revenues would mncrease due to a significant expansion of toll stations
throughout the Province, with tolls collected by local as well as by Provincial authorities.
A new road construction fund has been created, to be capitalized by fees on freight
transport (trucks), and based on vehicle ownership. Additional policies have been adopted
to generate funds in other sectors for infrastructure, or specifically highway, investment.
A special construction fund, maintained by the Province and eannarked for development
of high-grade highways, would be set up to meet futwe investment needs. The Provincial
government has also adopted a system of counterpart funding (grants), available for
repayment of investment loans related to a group of priority national and provincial roads.
A new infrastructure construction fee has been designed, applied as a 5 percent surtx
across various productive and consumer sectors. Finally, a 15-20 percent tax will be
levied on the sale of land adjacent to expressways, interchanges, and national or provincial
roads.
1S.         Fuel prices in China are not subsidized. Fuel taxes are charged to road
users, but are heavily levied by MOF, with the net result that such funds may not be
allocated back to road works. Taxes currently amount to 43 percent of the base price of
gasolines and 13 percent of the base price of diesel fuels. Total fuel prices, including
taxes, show that prices at the pump for planned fuel allocations are comparble to
international market pnces, while those charged in the open market are above international
market prices and are rising more rapidly than consumel prices (Appendix 2).
B. ENGEING, CoNSRucON AN MAINTANCE
16.         EngIneering. Highway works in the Province, including roads, bridges, and
tunnels, are mainly designed by the Fujian Provincial Transport Planning and Design
Insttute (FPPDI), a department within FPTD. Plans are reviewed by the Highway
Planning and Design Institute of MOC for major road and other engineering projects. The



- 83 -                        AX 2
First and Second Survey and Design Institutes in the Ministry of Railways may be involved
in the review of design documentation for major bridges and tunnels on behalf of MOC.
Small-scale road projects are engineered by the design departments at the prefectural,
municipal, or county levels. FPTPDI has a staff of 460. About 60 percent of the staff are
professional engineers, with the remaining staff in intennediate- and junior-level technical
and support functions. The institute's main worldng groups are specialized in the design
of highways, bridges, tunnels, waterways, and industrial and commercial buildings. Other
divisions in FPTPDI include a survey team, a geological survey team, a technology
information division, and a computer center. Computer applicat"   V are used in the design
process, specifically, through computer-aided design packages, pelmitting up to 80 percent
of the design work to be computerized.
17.        Construction. Some small-scale road and bridge projects are being directly
undertaken by the municipal/prefectual and county/district units of FPHAB. However,
larger new works, and, recently, most of the smaller ones are also being undertaken by
road construction enterprises, and FPHABWs activity by direct labor focuses largely on road
maintenance. Contts to road construction en'terpises are normally awarded under
competitive bidding procedures, although inviting a limited number of contractors to
submit bids is also being practiced. The present status of the provincial road construction
industry is discussed in Section E of this annex.
18.          ntenance. FPHAB, along with its regional bureaus at the prefectural/
municipal level and maintenance divisions at the county/district level, is responsible for
carrying out maintenance and rehabilitation of national, provincial, and local roads.
Maintenance of about 25 percent of the total loads in the Province is carried out by
highway bureau units at the municipality level. Maintenance of the remaining 75 percent
of the roads is administered through prefecturl highway bureau units (Appendix 3).
19.        The annual planning of maintenance works encompasses nine activities. The
most frequently performed maintenance activities comprise routine maintenance and
medium-term maintenance (resurfang). Major maintenance (rehabilitation of pavement)
and reconstruction (pavement construcdton, upgrading from sand to gavel, bitumen, or
cement surface, and alignment improvement) are carried out periodically. The plan also
provides for tasks such as planting of trees along tI.e roads, ferry maintenance, emergency
works, and maintenance of facilities, and procurement and maintenance of equipment.
20.         Schedules for major road maintenance activities are prepared on the basis
of road condition surveys throughout the Province. The data collected include the age,
degree of deterioration and the residual strength of pavement and the volume of traffic.
In practice, deterioration and strength of pavement have been the predominant factor in
maintenance planning. This system has been in place for nearly 10 years, and is now
consistently followed throughout the Province.
21.         In planning the work progrm  for routine maintenance each year, detailed
breakdowns of unit costs for pavement and maintenance works are used, costed in yuan
per km, including materials. For routine maintenance, the Province has maintined



-84-                          ANNEX 2
recerds on actual cost of work performed. A strict system is used for costing and design
of major maintenance and reconstruction works. For each such activity, a design plan with
breakdown of cost according to MOC guidelines must be followed. A review and approval
process is in place, followed by submission of a final costing report. Also, there is a
system in place for monitoring routine maintenance expenditures. In the last quarter of
each year, the budget plan is formulated, using targets whereby a certain percentage of
roads mt.,., be kept in a "good" condition classification. Both quality and financial
performance are monitored continuously, through regular reviews of work targets and
costs, complemented by inspections of roads. Such reviews are carried out on a monthly
basis for village roads, quarterly for county roads, and semiannually for provincial roads.
22.         Due to the predominantly low grade of pavements in the existing network
and the recent increase in traffic, pavement capacity is being stressed, with resultant
increases in maintenance and rehabilitation costs. As of 1992, the average cost of baseline
('routine") maintenance for all Provincial roads was Y 3,200 per kn per year for asphalt
roads, and Y 4,900 per km per year for gravel roads. The cost of maintaining national
or provincial roads is generally higher than the average for all roads, and in 1992 ranged
between Y 5,000 and Y 6,000 per km per year for routine maintenance. FPHAB also
provided subsidies of Y 400 to 600 per kn for the maintenance of local roads in 1992.
Additional costs of local road maintenance are funded through budgetary contlibutions of
transport bureaus at prefectural, municipal, county, and district levels.
23.         Although maintenance operations are well organized, certain deficiencies
have been identified that, if not addressed, could seriously undermine the effectiveness of
road maintenance operations over the long term. These include: (a) the structurally weak
pavement structures would require greater investment, specifically pavement strengthening;
(b) the road maintenance equipment fleet is largely outmoded and in poor condition; and
(c) the technology applied for maintenance is becoming obsolete. There are no easy and
readily available solutions to these problems. However, the formulation of a cohesive road
maintenance policy is advis .ble to prevent accelerated deterioration of the provincial
network that, according to MOC s standard evaluation criteria, is presently in fair-to-good
condition.

24.         The Education Division of FPTD  is responsible for the planning,
coordination and administration of training activities for the highway and waterway
subsectors. Formal training is mairly provided by (a) Fujian Provincial Highway
Vocational School, (b) Communications School; and (c) Communications Management
(Cader's) School, which are all under the administration of FPID.
25.         The Highway Vocational School trains junior middle-school graduates for
hee years primarily in skills required by automotive and construction equipment
mechanics and construction equipment operators in the field of road construction and
maintenance. It graduates 150 students every year, which provides grassroots technicians

for units of FPHAB and also for the provincial road construction enterprises and othe
reated industries.
26.        The Commwdcadons School tains intemediate-level technical and
management staff for FPMD and related agencies in the various prefectures, cities, and
counties in the field of highways and bridges, automobile servicing, financial management
and telecommunications. Consideration is being given to expand the curriculum to include
courses on contract implementation and management. The school requires an entry
examination and it graduates about 200 student a year from a four-year course.
27.         The Communwcadon Maiagemnt School provides training mainly for the
management-level staff of FPTD and related agencies in the various prefectues, cities and
counties, aimed at refesng/updating knowledge and skills regarding new policies,
technology and management. Training courses are nonmally of short- and medium-term
duration.
28.        Othetraining oppormnities in the highway field include: (a) intensive study
for senior highway personnel, such as directors and subdire s of city and prefecture

buraus at prefecture, municipality, and county levels.
29.        Trining opportnities for provincial highway personnel abroad have been
imited, although a number of taining programs were conducted abroad and in China
under the Bank's firs Highway Project with Fujian provincial staff participation. Also,
Staff of PPDI completed on-the-job training in England, Singapore and Hong Kong on
highway and bridge desgn.
30.         From the preceding it is evident that the highway institutions in the Province
have accorded high priority to training on a broad base, which has enhanced the technical
competence of te profesional staff presently on board. However, the exposure to modern
technology has still been limited and there is a recognized need for intensifying and
widening staff training efforts. The poposed project would provide further opportunities
for training, both in China and abroad to the personnel of FPTD, FPHAB (and its branches
in the prefectre, cities, and counties), FPECDO and FPTPDI in the fields of highway
planning, design, construcon, maintenace and operations, offering wider exposure to
technology and management of highways in other counties.
D. HIGIWAY SAliEY
31.         Since 1987, highway safety in China has been the responsibility of a Public
Security Bureau within each province. In Fujian, the Public Security Bureau's trffic
activities are ornized through provincial traffic police units. Police units deal directly
with taffic accidents, vehicle testing, drver licensing, and general traffic management,
including the provision of adequate traffic siging, road marldng, and installation of road



- 86 -                         NN       2
safety devices such as safety rails. Funding for the operation and administration of the
taffic police is provided by a fixed proportion of the annual RMP, which varies from
province to province. In Fujian Province, 3 percent of the RMF is directly alloated to
the Public Security Bureau.
32.         Licensing is strict, requiring a training test, a one-year practice period,
compledon of instruction in a driving school, and a road test. For truck drivers, one-half-
year full-time driving practice is required prior to licensing. The cost of obtaining a
driving license is considerable, at Y 2,000 for cars and Y 3,000 for trucks. About eight
different licenses are available for different operators. Measures are in place to limit the
operation of unsafe vehicles. All vehicles, including cars, trucks and motorcycles, must
be inpected each year at special inspection centers throughout the Province. Inspection
centers are up-to-date, including computerzed equipment capable of testing emissions and
noise. Traffic police may also perform limited inspections when vehicles sped.
33.         During the last few years the Public Security Bureau has taken the f.ollowing
specific measures to improve traffic management and road safety in the Province:
(a) reduced the number of fixed checkpoints that unjustiflably impeded the flow of taffic
and created public resentment; 0) replaced fixed checkpoint control of traffic with frequent
patrolling of heavily congested sections of the network with high risks of vehicle-to-vehicle
or vehicle-to-pedestrian or -bicycle conflicts; (c) conducted road safety campaigns through
the media (newspapers, radio, television) and courses about road safety in special dnver
education and public schools; and (d) introduced computeized accident recording systems,
which will eventually allow information to be shared with provincial transport authonties
and with a nationwide traffic data bank.
34.         The Province's Safety Commission, headed by a Vice-Govenor, meets
regularly to coordinate safety matters, including road safety. Although there are no formal
arrangements in place, FPHAB and municipal/prefecural transport authonties often consult
on traffic management and road safety matters and take joint actons as considered
approprate. Furthermore, the provincial road authorties ensure that adequate traffic
safety features are incorporated in the design of roads.
35.         While much has recently been accomplished to improve traffic management
and road safety in the Province, seeral problems remain to be addressed, including:
(a) laws and regulations are not well established to ensure consistency and equity in police
actions; (b) the education level and exerience of the police force are considered deficient,
which has a negative impact on police/public interaction; and (c) heavy congestion on some
secto  of the network, combined with undisciplined driver behavior, remains the major
cause of road traffic accidents.
E. RoAD CoNmwcnoN INRY
36.         Prior to 1986, all road works in Fujian Province were carried out by
construction teams within transport departments or units of provincial, municipal,
prefecturl, and county governments. Production targets were broadly set, however,



- 87 -                         ANNEX 2
completion of works was frequently delayed due to the operating inefficiency of contracting
units. The main factors contributing to inefficiency were the use of a design and build
approach, lack of clear contactual responsibility and absence of financial incentives. The
allocation of funds for road works was often inadequate, with the implicit understanding
that complementary funds would be forthcoming from general budgetary sources.
37.         In 1986, in line with economic reform efforts, two large and two medium-
sie road construction units were transformed into state-owned enterprises (SOEs). The
SOEs became financially independent, operating without budgetary support from the
govenment entities to which they were administratively attached. However, their
operations continue to be subjected to prevailing regulations, social obligations, and other
constraints that charcterize government entities in China. In addition to these four SOEs,
several collectively owned road construction units have been established in the Province
in recent years, which primarily work on lower-class provincial and municipal road
projects.
38.         At present, there are 17 enterprises in the Province licensed to undertake
road construction works, comprising 4 SOEs and 13 collectively owned enterprises. The
four SOEs operate on a province-wide basis. Six additional enterprises are designated as
municipal construction compenies, and the remaining seven enteprises as county
consucton companies. Thb enterpnses are based in cities and towns throughout the
Przovice, with the greatest *oncentration in the north-central region. Four enterpnses are
located along the eastern casdtine.
39.         The Provincial Construction Commission (PCC) witiin the Province of
Fujian is responsible for licensing and setting standards for all construction companies in
the Province. In the licensing process, the technical, managerial, and financial capacity
of each entepise is assessed, based on the type and size of projects undertaken, depth of
managerial and engineering experience, number of engineers, fixed assets, cash flow, and
gross income. Enterprises are then ranked on a scale from A to D, where the level of
classification designates the highest class of road an enterprise would be licensed to
constru     License A qualifies for Class 1 highways at the high end of the scale, and
License D for Class 4 roads at the low end of the scale. Distribution of the 17 enterprises
according to these licensing criteria is as follows: License A-2; License B-2;
License C-6; License D-7.
40.         The provincial construction industry is considered to be rather small, based
on the annual doll volume of works. For the five-year period, 1986-90, the two A-class
enterprises recorded an averge turnover of $6.0 million. The corresponding values for
the two B-class enterprises and the one C-class enterprise were $1.2 million and $1.0
millon, respectively. Applying these figures to the industry as a whole, the industry
turnover for the given period is estimated at about $25 million per year. However, the
capacity of the industry is believed to exceed this figure by about 30 percent. Regional
variation in work demand and limited mobility of the industial enterprises, particularly the
smaller ones, are considered to be the primary reasons for this.



- 88 -                         ANNEX 2
41.         Several external and intemal constraints facing the road construction industry
have been identified. These constraints adversely affect the performance of individual
enterprises, and have implications for the expansion of the entire industry on a competitive,
"iable basis. The major external constwnts include inappropriate costing standards applied
by employers for estimating the cost of construction contracts; administrative boundaries
restrict enterprises from other regions to participate in bidding; settng upper and lower
limits for accepting bids (bracketing), insufficient cost adjustment provisions to compensate
for price increases; the classification of potential construction works (i.e., major
reconstruction works) as maintenance which would then be carried out by government
force account units; lack of access to capital at a reasonable cost; the absence of an
established equipment leasing industry to accommodate the potential needs of enterprises,
particularly small ones; the routine use of incomplete contract drawings and specifications
which hinder the progress of works; and the fact that permissible profit margins for the
road construction industry are lower than other industries, such as manufacturing.
42.         The current inefficiency of the road construction industry has also resulted
from the internal constraints facing enterprises. Enterprises lack experience in preparing
bids and interpreting conditions of contracts such as Federation Internationale des
Ingenieurs-Conseils (FIDIC). Pricing of bids is, in fact, quite removed from cost recovery
or other internal considerations since enterprises are expected to match the employer's cost
estimates to secure a contract. Management autonomy and effective decision-making
within. ent.rprises are also constrained, with broad implications for the ability of
enterprises to respond to a changing environment, especially in the areas of organizational
stuu, technology, and financial management. Owners rather than line managers within
enterprises retain control in personnel, capital investment, operational, and certain
administative matters, weakening efforts to pursue a more market-oriented operation.
43.         The confluence of external and internal constraints, as described above, has
also had a negative impact on the acquisition of essential assets, such as equipment, and
modern management sklls by the road construction enterpnses. The general level of
technology among the enterprises is insufficient, charcterized by a shortage of equipment,
particularly for earthworks and pavement works, but also of laboratory and field testing
equipment used in quality control. The enterprises also continue to suffer from a lack of
exposure to up-to-date construction technology. The management of enterprises needs to
be strengthened, particularly in the areas of equipment maintenance and utilization, and in
the planning, on-site organization, scheduling and controlling of works.
44.         The financial performance and viability of road construction enterprises is
threatened by fluctuating workloads, low profit margins, and, in certain cases, significant
leverage. Fluctuations in turnover appear significant, on the order of plus or minus
30-40 percent per year, depending on the volatility of demand. Annual proflte are low,
estimated at 3 percent of urnover. Some enterprises have made effective use of financial
leverage; the four larger enterprises, the two A-class enterprises recorded liabilities equal
to 37 percent and 52 percent of total assets, respectively, and the liability to asset ratos
for the two B-class enterprises were 37 percent and 8 percent. However, cash flow
management in the firms remains weak, which poses risks for liability management.



- 89 -                        ANNEX2
Effective financial management is furher complicated by the limited advance payment for
mobilztion of works, lack of cost-based compettion in contract bidding, and severely
restricted access to capital. More generally, the entprises seem to have very limited
experence in many critical areas of finacial management, such as pncing, costing,
planning and budgeting, and accounting.
45.        Directives regarding the tansiton of the operating mechanisms of SOEs are
currently being formulated at both national and provincia levels. Fujian Province recently
prepared guidelines and revised regulations that would be applied to all provincial SOEs,
including the road construction enterprises. The proposed measures are expected to
become, eventually, beneficial to the SOEs in the road construction industry by increasing
management autonomy in the SOEs and a stronger nrwket orientation. However, these
measures alone will not bring about the required level of change in the industry, and must
be accompanied by other measures. The project, therefore, includes a detailed study of
the provincial road industry, which will compnse an in-depth diagnosis of existng
conditions and the preparation of recommendations and an acdon plan intended to pro' ide
a basis for overall strengthening of the industry.
